This report is based on the findings of an appraisal mission which visited
Uruguay from April 11 to 23, 1988. The mission was composed of Messrs./Mme.
Marta Rios, Task Manager and Financial Analyst; Sergio Miouel, Highway
Engineer; Gustavo Unda, Port Engineer; Peter Gyamfi, Economist; and Noor
Rasheed, Financial Analyst. Messrs. Bob Sisson and Luis Pisani assisted the
mission in highway engineering and training aspects, respectively. Mesdames
Leonor Santelices and Brenda Turner assisted in the production oi this report.
This document has a restricted distribution and may be used by recipients only in the performance
of their oficial duties. Its contents may not otherwise be disclosed without World Bank authorization.

URUGUAY
TRANSPORT PROJECT I
I.  LOAti AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrow^r:      Republica Oriental del Uruguay
Executing      Ministry of Transport and Public Works (MTOP) and
Agencies:      National Port Administration (ANP)
Amount:         US$80.8 million equivalent
Terms:         Repayment in 15  years,  including  5  years  of  grace, a:: the
Bank's standard variable rate.
Project
Description:   The project includes high  priority investments, sector reforms
and institutional developmient Action Plans for the roads and
ports subsector agencies.   The major investments would include
the following: (a)   roads:    a highway replacement program; a
time-slice  of  MTOP's   road  rehabilitation  and  maintenance
program; and a departmental gravel roads program; (b) ports:
construction  of  a   dike   to  reduce  maintenance  dredging,
remodeling of the commercial area, upgrading of port utilities
and power supply systems, and provision of cargo handling
equipment, all the above at Montevideo port; construction of a
second breakwater and a water supply system, and dredging to
expand the access channel and the basin at the Paloma fishing
port; expansion of berthing facilities at Punta del Este small
craft port; extension of existing breakwater and construction
of berthing and boat-repair facilities at Piriapolis small
craft port; and (c) technical assistance and training to
support the implementation of the Action Plans. The Action
Plans would  include  measures  to:    (a) strengthen transport
planning  and  adjust   sector   policies;   (b)  improve  road
management  practices;   and   (c)   increase   port  operating
efficiency.
Benefits:      Financial benefits  would  include  the  reduction and eventual
elimination of the ports deficits and the preservation of the
capital stock in the roads subsector. Economic benefits wiould
be lower transport and tranFfer costs, savings from deferring
investments and increased foreign exchange earnings.
Risks and
Safeguards:    The project  would  cover  the  roads  and  port subsectors and
involve several agencies.   There  's,  therefore, risk of slow
project implementation, for which a Project Coordinating Unit
would be established within HTOP to coordinate implementation
and monitor progress. Also, the project's main thrust would be
the far-reaching sector policy and institutional reforms. Some
risk exists that the next Government may falter in carrying out
the agreed reforms. In order to lessen that possibility, the



- 2 -
project  arrangements  include   provisions  for  joint  annual
reviews to enable the Bank to continue to have an effective
dialogue on the implementation of the reforms.

II. THE TRANSPORT SECTOR
A.   General
2.01      Uruguay's transport infrastructure comprises  62,000 km of roads, of
which 11Z are paved; 3,000 km of railway track; one deep water port and a
large number of minor ports; an off-shore tanker mooring buoy and pipelines
for petroleum imports; and 25 commercial airports. Montevideo, Uruguay's main
commercial and population center, is the hub of the transport system and the
main foreign trade  port.    Although  the  transport system provides adequate
access to most areas, it is old and inadequate for present and future needs.
Most roads were built over 30 years ago and very little investments have been
made in ports and railways over the past decade. Roads are the predominant
domestic transport mode accounting for over 87Z of total domestic freight
(ton-km), excluding petroleum and its subproducts, and 92Z of non-urban
passenger traffic (passenger-km).   Value  added  by  the transport sector has
remained at about 62 of GDP for the last 25 years.
2.02      A favorable assessment  of  Uruguay's  fish  resource potential made
with FAO's assistance in 1975/76 resulted in Government's decision to develop
the industry and La Paloma as an industrial fishing port. The Government is
pursuing more intensive development of the fisheries sector through incentives
to the private sector and to the La Paloma port, which is expected to be
served by three private fish processing plants.
2.03      Tourism, an important element of the  Uruguayan economy, accounts in
some years for as much as 172 of export earnings. During the peak 1985 season
more than 450,000 tourists visited Uruguay and spent more than US$111 million,
equivalent to about 13% of the value of goods exported during the year. The
peak tourist traffic substantially exceeds the capacity of t'e access roads
and ports, reoacing the level of service and user safety.
2.04      The principal role of the transport sector for the next decade would
be to support agricultural and industrial production, increased fisheries
development and the expanding tourism  demand.    Investments in the sector in
the short to medium-term should emphasize: (a) the improvement, rehabilitation
and maintenance of neglected infrastructure and equipment; (b) modernization
of transport facilities and services including particularly the improvement in
the management and operations of the state enterprises; (c) the provision of
additional capacity to accommodate future fisheries and tourism demand; and
(d)  the   increase   in   private   sector   participation.          The  present
administration's program for the transport sector (para. 2.29) is based on the
preceding objectives.
B.   Institutional Framework
2.05      The  Ministry   of   Transport   and   Public   Works   (MTOP),  has
responsibility for developing  the  national  transport  policy, for transport
planning and coordination, for setting  tariffs for national and international
transport services, for registering all transport equipment, and for studyik,g
and implementing all  national  public  works.    Through  its Directorates of
Transport (DNT), of Roads (DNV) and of Hydrography (DNH), MTOP oversees
national and international rail, air, road ard shipping services and the
national roads and small craft ports infrastructure. Regional services and



- 4 -
small craft ports infrastructure.    Regional  services ond infrastructure are
the direct  responsibility  of  the  Departmental  Governments.   Three public
sector enterprises: the State Railway Administration (AFE), the National Ports
Administration (ANP) and the First T'ruguayan Airline (PLUNA) administer and
provide  transport  services   in   their   respective   modes.         The  above
organizational arrangements are adequate in principle. In practice, however,
MTOP has had little involvement with ANP and AFE, intezmodal planning and
coordination are weak, (para. 2.06), and the respective roles of MTOP and
other public agencies involved with small craft and fishing ports are unclear
ancl uncoordinated (paras. 2.15 and 2.16).
C.   Main Issues
(i) Sector-wide Issues
2.06      MTOP's Investment  Planning.    The  institutional  organization for
investment programming needs strengthening.   The  Office for Planning and the
Budget (OPP) which has traditionally provided guidelines to public enterprises
by authorizing investment levels, external financing and approving tariff
levels, has recently expanded its efforts and set development objectives and
project evaluation guidelines.   This  needs  to  be  supported by a parallel,
substantial improvement in the sectoral  programming  units.  In the transport
sector, MTOP is by statute responsible for putting together transport plat1:.
for monitoring implementation, and for annual revisions to reflect changing
needs and resources. Planning units within the modal agencies are expected to
identify and evaluate investments in their respective modes as an input to
MTOP's sector-wide transport plan.    However, MTOP's planning performance has
traditionally been weak due to: (a) Insufficient commitment to planning and
inadequate in-house planning capacity; (b) insufficient technical support from
OPP; and (c) weak planning capacity in the modal agencies. MTOP has made a
good start with the creation of its Transport Planning and Coordinating Unit
recently renamed Technical Economic Evaluation Unit (AETE), which has so far
established a transport data base and been assisting DNV in screening its road
projects. To further strengthen its planning and coordinating capacity, MTOP
plans to take the following actions: (a) extend AETE's role to ir._lude
preparation of pluriannual transport plans and their annual revision; (b)
develop the necessary planning tools at the ministerial level; and (c) assist
the modal agencies  to  strengthen  planning  capacities.    The project would
provide technical assistance to MTOP for strengthening AETE in its expanded
planning role, by helping develop the necessary planning methodologies and
tools and in training staff.   MTOP's  Action  Plan (para. 3.08(a) and (b) and
Annex 1] for strengthening its planning and coordinating capacity, with an
outline of specific actions, the responsibility for taking them and their
timing, was confirmed during negotiations.
2.07      Road User Charges.  Because of  lack of adequate data collection and
processing systems, information on revenues from road user charges in Uruguay
is unreliable. The Bank's estimates, based upon traffic flows, motor fuel
consumption, and the road user charges in force, indicate revenues of about
TIS$76 million compared to about US$60 million needed annually for adequate
periodic and routine maintenance and administration of the highway network.
There is, therefore, no economic reason to adjust the charges at this time.
Also, the price3 of gasoline and diesel-oil at US$1.70 and US$1.09 per gallon,
respectively, are higher than the border prices plus production costs. MTOP,
however, needs to establish a better information base of road-user charge



-5 -
rcceipts and a mechanism for systematic updating of user charge levels and for
monitoring their adequacy in relation to the road network's marginal costs in
order to permit  proposing  adjustments  when  necessary.    The project would
provide technical assistance to MTOP to carry out a more reliable comparison
of road-user charges receipts and to set up the mechanism for the systematic
monitoring of the adequacy of user charge levels. MTOP's Action Plan [para.
3.08(c) and Annex 1) details the actions to be taken and the schedule for
their implementatior.. During negotiations, the Government agreed to complete
the updated user charges revenue study by October 31, 1989, to discuss it with
the Bank and to make any necessary adjustments to the levels of charges to
ensure that user charges reflect at least the short-term marginal costs of
maintaining and administering the network by December 31, 1989.
(ii) Highways Subsector Issues
2.08      The detailed description of  the  highways  subsector is provided in
Annex  1.    The  following  paragraphs  concentrate  on  the  DNV's  and  the
departmental agencies' main issues and areas of institutional weaknesses.
2.09      DNV's  Highway  Planning.    Since  1985  DNV  has  made significant
progress in introducing economic criteria for investment selection. The first
step was made by screening, on the basis of economic rates of return, about
200 road projects and organizing the justifiable projects in a pluriannual
road investment and maintenance  program.   For foreign-financed road projects
under the program, DNV has been updating the engineering and economic
feasibility studies using consultants, its own staff or AETE. For those
projects locally financed (up to 602 of the program) only the cost estimates
are updated. The strengthening of AETE's planning and coordinating capacity
to cover all transport modes (para. 2.06) would help eliminate weaknesses in
the highway planning process.    Under  the  project,  AETE would, inter alia,
prepare a rolling Five-Year Road Investment and Maintenance Plan more closely
linked with other sectors of the economy and would carry out annual reviews of
the Plan. The project would provide for technical assistance to AETE to
improve its existing methods and too.s for developing the Road Plan. The
specific actions to be ta'..en to this end and their schedule are detailed in
the MTOP's Action Plan for roads [para. 3.09(s) and Annex 1] agreed with the
Bank.
2.10      Administration of  the  Road  Network:  DNV  is  responsible for the
administration, planning, construction and maintenance of the 9,700 km of
national roads (of which about 70% are paved) while the departmental road
agencies are responsible for about 52,700 km of departmental roads. The
existing road classification needs updating to reflect some significant
changes in traffic levels  and  function  of  some  roads.   The project would
provide technical assistance for this purpose, based on which DNV would make
proposals  for   the   necessary   Government   legislation   to   effect  the
riclassification.  The Government  agreed  on  these  measures, as outlined in
MTOP's Action Plan for roads [para. 3.09(b) and Annex 1].
2.11      DNV's  and  Departmental  Agencies'  Technical  Capacity.    Lack of
sufficient technical capacity has been one of the main reasons for DNV's and
the departments' lese than satizfartory performance in carrying out the major
highway related functions, particularly construction and maintenance. The
project would provide technical assistance particularly in the areas of
engineering, quality control and cost control to DNV and the departments
through  the  establishment  of   computerized  methods  of  design,  improved
laboratory facilities and personnel  training.    MTOP's Action Plan for roads



[para. 3.09(c) and Annex 1] outlines the implementation schedule for these
reforms.
2.12      Operation of  the  Highway  Network.    DNV's present organizational
structure does not include a traffic department capable of applying the
necessary engineering methodologies to provide efficient traffic operation and
adequate safety levels. Considerable improvement has been ac.lieved by AETE,
which since 1983 has been conducting a traffic counting program as well as
origin and destination surveys used mairly as an input for economic evaluation
of projects. However, additional technical assistance is required. Under
MTOP's Action Plan for roads [para. 3.09(d) and Annex 1], DNV would establish
a traffic division or department capable of prov'ding for traffic operations,
traffic engineering,  safety  surveys  and  related  studies.    The technical
assistance to be contracted  under  the  project would support this initiative
through the establishment of a  continuous system for gathering and evaluating
road accident data, and by improving design standards and deployment of
traffic control devices.
2.13      Maintenance Management.   Maintenance  of  all  national highways is
performed exclusively by DNV force account. Routine maintenance is generally
satisfactory, but the combination of increasing costs, inadequate periodic
maintenance and a highway network approaching 40 years of age, requires
additional effort to maintain an acceptable level of service. Development of
an effective maintenance management system has a high priority for DNV at this
time. Maintenance on the departmental roads is much worse. Although each
departmental government has an office responsible for public buildings and
roads, understaffing, lack of technical capability and shortage of funds have
made these units  ineffective.    In  addition,  design,  and construction and
maintenance practices are not standardized between the departments and, for
the most part, they do not apply DNV's national network standards. MTOP's
Action Plan for roads [para. 3.09(e) -.nd Annex 1] includes an upgrading of
DNV's  maintenance  directorate  as  well   as  the  transfer  of  maintenance
technologies to the departments. The project would be providing the necessary
technical assistance to support these initiatives.
(iii) Ports Subsector Issues
2.14      Details of the ports subsector  are  provided  in Annex 3.  The main
issues in the subsector are discussed below.
2.15      Ports St.  ector Institutional  Framework.    A multitude of agencies
are involved in ports administration, operation and marketing, including:
MTOP, through DNH and DNT; ANP; the Ministry of Labor and Social Security,
through the National Administration for Stevedore Sertices (ANSE); the
Ministry of Economy and Finance, through the customs, foreign trade regul-
ations and naval industries; the Ministry of Defense and the Navy, through the
National Coast Guard (PNN); and the Ministry of Agriculture, through the
National Institute of  Fishing  (INAPE).    In  addition, the recently-created
Ministry of Tourism is expected to be involved in promoting small craft ports
and harbours. In general, a case exists for the presence of all these agen-
cies in the ports subsector, but the areas of authority for each of these
agencies needs to be defined. Moreover, as explained below, some agencies are
performing functions that could be more efficiently handled by the private
sector.



-7-
2.16      ANP is an autonomous  state  enterprise respcnsible for the adminis-
tration and operation of all commercial ports incLuding cargo-handling (except
stevedoring which is carried out by ANSE), towage, dredging, port construction
and maintenance, operation of some old merchant vessels and ship repair
facilities, and  granting  of  licences  for  port  servicrs.   The Government
recognizes that this dispersion of ANP's efforts over too many areas, is a
major contributor to its low productivity. DNH is the directorate within MTOP
responsible for  constructing  and  operat4ng  all  small  craft harbours, for
operating the fishing port of La Paloma, for rnaintaining navigable waterways
and buoy markings, for the provision  of ferry and river crossing services and
for the promotion of small craft  ports.   The Government also recognizes that
DNH's role should be more  limited,  pzobably  to  the purely technical one of
planning, building and maintaining the small craft Dorts and navigable
waterways.  In particular, the  promotion  and  operation of small craft ports
are tasks better suited for the private sector or the Ministry of Tourism, and
the operation of fishing ports should be placed elsewhere. Under the proposed
project the Government would review and decide on the specific role of each
public agency and of the private sector and recommend enactment of the
necessary laws or decrees to assign the associated responsibilities. The
Government  would  also  study   ways   for  promoting  wider  private  sector
participation in the development and operation of small craft ports and in the
provision of dredging services.   The  specific  actions to be taken and their
timing to remedy the Institutional framework for ports administration and
operation is provided in the MTOP's Action Plan for ports [para. 3.10(a) and
Annex 1] agreed with the Bank.
2.17      ANP's Organization and  Functions.    ANP  has  an obsolete organiz-
ational structure, is overstaffed and not geared to a cohirnercially oriented
operation. Moreover, although nominally autonomous, ANP's decision making has
been undermined by the intervention in its day-to-day operations by its Board
of Directors. Coordination of ANP's port operation activities with tnose of
ANSE, which carries out the stevedoring in the Montevideo port, is also weak.
Following the Government's decision to reexamine ANP's role, MTOP and ANP with
consultants assistance to ', provided under the project, would carry out an
in-depth review of '.e s organization, operational procedures and methods and
required staffing. This review would also include the upgrading of ANP's
marketing   and   commercial   research    capabilities   to   define   market
characteristics, clients' needs and competition factors. The Government would
then set up a revised organizational structure for ANP aimed at making it more
commercially oriented. The implementation schedule for these reforms is also
outlined in MTOP's Action Plan for ports [para. 3.10(b) and (c) and Annex 1].
2.18      Planning and Budgeting.  ANP  also  suffers from weak in-house capa-
city for inv"stment planning  and  pluriannual  budgeting.   The recently com-
pleted Master Plan for the Montevideo port provides a useful basis for the
port's short to long-term development  program.   Under the MTOP's Action Plan
for ports [para. 3.10(d) and Annex 1], ANP would strengthen its capacity to
update the Plan's recommendations annually to reflect changing port demand,
availability of resources and productivity changes. ANP would also institute
and revise as necessary, the system for pluriannual budgeting of its activi-
ties designed by consultants under the last port project (Loan 1798-UR), and
train its statf in its use. The project would provide the necessary technical
assistance to support the preceding measures.



2.19      Port Operations.  Productivity  at  Montevideo  port ia low compared
with that of other ports of similar size and volume of traffic in developing
countries.    Analysis  shows  that  both  cargo  and  gang  productivity  are
considerably lower than that of other ports, and that the idle time of ships
alongside berths of the Montevideo port is considerable longer than in other
ports. A detailed analysis of the causes of this situation is included in
Annex 3. Low productivity is the result of lack of coordination between
stevedoring and on-shore cargo handling, inappropriate cargo handling methods,
lack of incentives for efficiency, inadequate workers' skills, poor equipment
maintenance and inadequate storage areas.   ANP, with consultants' assistance,
intends to: (a) reorganize the port into specialized terminals, with their own
personnel and equipment for containers, general cargo, perishable goods and
fishing services; (b) develop and adopt an efficiency-indicator system to
monitor port productivity; (c) introduce a productivity-based compensation
system for workers; (d) improve coordination of stevedoring and on-shore cargo
handling; (e) train port operations staff; and (f) remove physical constraints
such as lack of ineependent access to the container terminal, obsolete sheds,
deficient internal electric systems to operate container and quay cranes and
inadequate port handling equipment.   These  actions would be undertaken under
the proposed project, and their scheduling is outlined in MTOP's Action Plan
for ports (para. 3.10(e) and (f) and Annex 11. Necessary technical assistance
to support these measures is also being provided under the project.
2.20      Equipment  Maintenance:  ANP  has  no  systematic  approach  towards
equipment maintenance. Analytical maintenance planning as well as preventive
and periodic equipment maintenance are practically non-existent. In addition,
annual budgets bear no  relationship  to  needs.    As  outlined in the MTOP's
Action Plan for ports  (para.3.10(g)  and  Annex  1],  ANP would undertake the
following actions to improve its equipment maintenance: (a) establish, with
consultants'  assistance  a  system  for  planning,  executing  and monitoring
equipment  maintenance;  (b)  review   and  adapt  its  equipment  maintenance
organization and provide training for  staff  to implement the system; and (c)
allocate sufficient funds in its p.uriannual budgets for equipment maintenance
based upon a detailed program.
2.21      Financial  Management.    ANP's  financial  planning,  budgeting and
accounting systems, which are being upgraded with consultants' assistan-e
under L3an 1798-UR, will allow a more effective programming and subsequent
control of ANP's operations as well as more realistic planning of its capital
expenditures.   Follow  up  measures,  including  the  computerization  of the
systems and the establishment of internal audit functions, are part of MTOP's
Action Plan for ports (para. 3.10(h) and Annex 1]. DNH, as part of a central
government agency, operates under the five year budget approved by Congress.
As with the rest of MTOP's directorates, DNH budgeting as well as accounting
systems wculd need to  be  strengthened.    MTOP's  Action Plan for ports also
includes the upgrading of DNH's financial management system.
2.22      Costs and Tariffs.  ANP's tariff  system, because of lack of system-
atic cost data, has no relation to costs and therefore, a gredt number of
tariffs are quoted on an ad-valorem  basis.    All charges are expressed in US
dollars, as a proxy for automatic adjustment to currency variations. There
are other disparities in the existing tariff policy comprising: (a) lack of
uniform export tariffs at all ports; (b) lack of charges for the use of quay
cranes: (c) import tariffs more than double export tariffs; and (d) numerous
tarift exemptions for fishing vessels.   Under the previous port project (Loan



- 9 -
1798-UR), consultants designed a sound port costing system by cost center and
service, which is scheduled to be fully instituted by end-1988. The tourist
ports new tariffs include charges per meter length and day as well as for
utilities  and  other  services.         Punta   del  Este  also  applies  tariff
differentials depending on the berth and the season (high, mid and low), which
appears logical. It would be better, however, if all tariffs were cost-based.
The Action Plan for the ports [para. 3.10(g) and Annex 11 includes a revision
of ANP's and the small craft ports' tariff structure based on the new costing
system to ensure cost recovery.
2.23      ANP's Financial Performance.    ANP's  financial  evaluation of ANP,
pre~sented in Annex 4, indicates a deteriorating financial situation since
1980.    Declining  traffic,  increasing  staff  costs,  low  productivity and
inadequate tariff adjustments have gradually eroded the entity's financial
capacity, turning, in 1984, net profits into net losses. In addition, as a
result of the deteriorating operating results and of increasing foreign debt
obligations, ANP's cash  position  has  become  critical.    Since 1983 it has
resorted to new bank loans to service existing debt obligations, further
aggravating the cash deficits. A financial forecast for 1988-1995, based upon
the proposed reorganization of ANP (para. 2.17), staff reductions, tariff
revision and expected productivity increases show an improvement in working
and operating ratios, from 86 and 103 in 1987 to 61 and 79 in 1995 and in
financial rate of return on revalued fixed assets from -0.62 in 1988 to 4X in
1995. In order to ensure ANP's financial recovery, the following agreements
were reached at negotiations: (a) the Government would provide the necessary
financial support to ANP totaling about US$20 million in the 1988-1989
period, to cover its external debt service payments; (b) ANP would restructure
its tariffs based on the new costing system, by March 30, 1990; (c) ANP would
not incur any debt, in addition to the proposed loan unless its net operating
revenues are at least 1.5 times the annual debt service charges; (d) ANP would
achieve a ratio of operating expenses to operating revenues not higher than
87? in 1990, 842 in 1991, 82? in 1992, 802 in 1993 and 1994 and 792 in 1995
and thereafter; and (e) ANP would achieve financial rates of return on
revalued fixed assets of not less than 2.0X in 1990, 2.62 in 1991, 2.92 in
1992, 3.32 in 1993, 3.72 in 1994 and 4.0 in 1995 and thereafter.
2.24      Financial Viability of La  Paloma  Port.    At present the La Paloma
port does not generate any revenue. MTOP has been absorbing total capital and
operating costs through the Government budget.   It should be the objective of
the port to achieve cost recovery of port operations through user charges.
ASTRA, the only one of the three fish factories in the area which is presently
in operation, was granted special incentives (port tariff and tax exemptions)
during the start-up period. These exemptions are due to expire in 1991. La
Palor.a port, however, would only achieve financial viability if a second fish
factory of similar size as ASTRA starts operations in the area. Project
investments in the port would, therefore, be contingent upon confirmation of
the start of operations of second fish processing factory [paras. 2.31 and
3.06 (d)].
2.25      Financial Viability of Piriapolis  Port.  Presently, the Piriapolis'
meagre income does not cover its operating costs. TVe Government's budget for
MTOP provides for the capital and recurrent expenditute. Through the project,
this port should ultimately become financially viable (Annex 4). The main
sources of income will be 1he  adequtiate port charges for the services provided
and the regular ttansfer of funds  t.' tlip lptt rpstilt itig from the appreciation



- 10 -
of land and property. The critical factor for the financial viability of the
port is that the revenues from the leasing of land to private concessionaires,
and the appreciation of land and property value in the area, continue to be
passed  on  to  the  Piriapolis   port.       The  Government  should  take  the
responsibility  to  ensure  such  revenues  are  forthcoming.    Assurance was
obtained from the Government that it would therefore guarantee that it will
provide a financial contribution which would make up for any shortages from
loss of income from leasing of lanu and appreciation of property values in the
port area.
(vi) Railway Subsector Issues
2.26      The railway subsector  has  faced  serious  problems for many years.
Demand for rai±way services has virtually stagnated in the last decade, there
has been little investment since 1952 and the company's financial situation
has recently required annual Government subsidies of USS20-30 million. In
light  of  this  situation,  in   1987  the  Government  designed  a  complete
rationalization of railway  services.    This  program,  which includes, inter
alia, a complete reorganization of the enterprise and reductions in service,
lines  and  employees  is   reflected  in  a  Government-Railways  performance
contract. Upon obtaining legislative approval, the Government in January 1988
embarked on a series of actions aimed at downsizing the railway to fit its
present and future economic role. Although the most important measures of the
rationalization program were designed to be carried out over a five-year
period (1988-1992), the results of the first year, which include, inter alia,
elimination of passenger and parcel services and declaration of 28Z of AFE's
staff redundant, will most likely exceed the targets, demonstrating clearly
Government's commitment to  these  commendable  and  difficult   reforms.  The
proposed project will not deal with the railway subsector, in light of the
advanced stage of the  rationalization  program.    However, the Bank would be
regularly informed on the course of the program.
2.27      Railway Rationalization  Measures.    Until  January  1988, when the
Government terminated passenger transt 't on the railways, AFE provided
freight car load services on all of its 2,987 kms of lines and passenger
services on all except  three  small  lines.    Freight  traffic demand on the
network is so low that even if AFE took the necessary steps to improve its
productivity,  maintenance,  planning  and   financial  management,  it  would
continue to be both financially and economically non-viable if it kept all of
its present network.   A  recent  independent  study of AFE's future prospects
concluded that the railways could be viable in the medium to long term for
freight traffic if: (a) it reduced the length of its network by at least 40Z;
(b)  focussed  its  services  on   block-train  and  wagon-load  traffic;  (c)
refurbished its  remaining  track  and  equipment;  and  (d) reduced  staff to
correspond to  the  above  strategy.    As  stated  above,  the Government has
approved these recommendations and designed a complete restructuring of AFE,
which is currently underway.    In  addition,  AFE  has provided for technical
assistance (about US$2.3 million) in its 1988-1990 investment program to help
improve  administration,  planning,  maintenance  and  signalization.    AFE's
programmed recruitment of consultants would also assist in introducing a
proper financial management  sys.em.    Active  involvement  of MTOP and AFE's
Board in the railways management is expected to continue during the next few
years when AFE will still be undergoing its major restructuring.



- 11 -
D.   MTOP's Transport Sector Expenditure Program
2.28      MTOP's transport expenditure  program  encompasses: rehabilitating a
primary highway (Route 1) which is part of the tourism network, building roads
in the agricultural areas to improve access to processing facilities and
improving maintenance of the rest of the road network; modernizing and
improving infrastructure in the Montevideo port, La Paloma fishing port and
several tourist ports, including Punta del Este; streamlining the railway and
improving the remaining track and rolling stock; and adapting air transport
services to  the  country's  needs.    The  transport  expenditure  program is
generally in line with the country's needs, as discussed below.
2.29      The 1987-1989 public  sector  expenditure  program  for transport is
presented in Annex 5, Table 1.   The overall size of the program, about US$317
million or 1.52 of GDP and 30% of the total public sector investments for the
period, is appropriate, particularly considering the past neglect of the
transport infrastructu-e and current and future needs. The program is also in
line with the ceilings authorized by the Office for Planning and Budget (OPP)
in March 1988.   The  relative  amounts  allocated  to  the various modes also
correctly reflect their economic role and needs: about 74% of the total is for
highways, 15% for ports, 7Z for railways, 1% for civil aviation and 3X for
overseeing transport operations and regulations. For highways, about one-half
of the program would be for maintenance which is both appropriate and in
proportion  to  DNV's  implementation  capacity.    The  investments  in  road
construction and rehabilitation are based on carefully analyzed economic
feasibility and priority ranking.    For  ANP,  the  1987-1989 program for the
M1ontevideo port, about US$7 million, is in line with short to medium-term
needs and would comprise high priority, economically justified investments
recommended by the Bank-supervised Montevideo Port Master Plan Study. DNH's
investments, on the other hand, include some tourist ports such as Puerto
Viejo de Colonia and Atlantida for which economic justification has not yet
been clearly established.    The  US$22  million  planned expenditures on AFE,
which take into consideration Government's policy reforms and restructuring of
the railways, comprise needed track renewal and rehabilitation, overhaul of
locomotives,  installation  of  a   new  signalization  system  and  technical
assistance  for  implementing   organizational   reforms   and  for  improving
management and operations. AFE's 1987-89 investments have been evaluated by
consultants and found  economically  feasible.    The planned expenditures for
civil aviation, essentially for PLUNA, are minimal and have not yet been
economically evaluated. Implementation of the first year of the 1987-1989
public sector program reached only about 50% for the transport sector
agencies, except DNv which exceeded its program slightly.
2.30      The transport agencies are currently preparing expenditure proposals
for 1988 to 1992. Preliminary estimates from their draft proposals call for
about US$690 million over the five year period, or an average of US$138
million p.a., which is about 302 above the OPP-approved yearly averages for
1987-89 (Annex 5, Table 1).    Therefore,  although  about 90% of the proposed
expenditures have already been justified economically, OPP would need to
review carefully and revise the individual modal programs to ensure that they
are in line with the agencies' implementation capacities and the availability
of funds to the  overall  nublic  sector.    In  fact, the expenditure program
represents a pluri-annual authorization to incur expenditures as well as an
expenditure ceiling authorized by the Parliament, while actual expenditures
are subject to reductions based on annual authorized budgets. Specific



- 12 -
comments on the individual modal programs for 1988-92 are given in Annex 5.
At negotiations, the size and composition of MTOP's 1988-1992 Transport
Expenditure Program was reviewed and, in addition, the Government agreed to
inform the Bank on each updating of the Transport Sector Expenditure Program
during project implementation.
E.   Bank Involvement in the Sector and Lending Strategy
(i) Past Involvement
2.31      The Bank has played an important role in Uruguay's transport system.
The first highway loan in 1963, financed improvements to Route 5 and, in 1979,
a second loan financed improvements along the Route 8 corridor. A port loan,
also in 1979, financed the conversion of a wharf at the Montevideo Port into a
container cargo terminal and the provision of floating equipment for the
container terminal and floating craft including dredging equipment. The most
recent highway loan, in 1983, is financing part of a five year road investment
and maintenance program. It was revised in 1986 to increase its support for
maintenance activities. Physical objectives of the completed projects were
satisfactorily achieved. Institution-building of past lending has focused on
training staff to carry out feasibility studies, mainly for road projects and
for financial control in ANP.    Improved maintenance management practices for
road infrastructure and equipment have been successfully introduced, but need
to be developed further.
(ii) Lending Strategy
2.32      The Bank's general lending  strategy  in  Uruguay  is to continue to
support policy reforms while providing for investments in projects which
address  specific  bottlenecks.    In   the  transport  sector,  the  specific
objectives are to assist in the improvement, rehabilitation and maintenance of
existing facilities, improve management and operational performance of the
sector's agencies and to help resolve the key policy issues. The proposed
project would be used to support policy reforms, to address some of the issues
and provide financing for priority  investment  needs.   At the same time, the
project would support efforts to strengthen sector and subsector management
and to increase private sector participation in the sector. The institutional
policy reforms are included in MTOP's Action Plans to be implemented under the
project.
III. THE PROJECT AND THE ACTION PLANS
A.    Project Objectives and Content
3.01      The proposed project is  designed  as  a  sector project with strong
policy-based objectives aimed at reforming the sector, strengthening its
managerial and planning capabilities and stimulating competition and private
sector involvement. It includes institutional development Action Plans for
MTOP and ANP and provides for high priority investments in the roads and ports
subsectors. In particular, the Action Plans would aim at: (a) strengthening
road management capability, especially maintenance planning and programming of
MTOP  and  the  transfer   of   know-how   to  departmental  governments;  (b)
reorganizing the ports subsector, including the redefinition of functions and



- 13 -
responsibilities for planning, constructing, maintaining and operating the
ports;  and  (c)  improving   transport   regulations,  tariffs  and  reducing
subsidies. The investments would help finance: a highway replacement program,
a time-slice of MTOP's road rehabilitation and maintenance program and a
departmental gravel roads program; priority rehabilitation works in ports;
expansion of La Paloma fishing port and Punta del Este and Piriapolis small
crLft ports; ..,d an institutional development program including technical
assistance and training to support the implementation of the Action Plans.
B.   Rationale for Bank Involvement
3.02      The Bank's strategy for  Uruguay  emphasizes supporting reforms that
enhance Uruguay's balance of payments prospects through increased exports, and
assisting the Government's adjustment program by, among inter alia, reducing
the fiscal deficit. Major reforms are now called for in the transport sector
to support such a  strategy:    transport  facilities, services and management
procedures need to be modernized to meet present and forecast demand;
transport costs need to be reduced in order to help promote foreign trade,
strengthen regional integration and facilitate domestic market growth; and
parastatals such as ANP, need to eliminate their deficits and contribute to
national savings. Through its earlier loans for highways and for ports, the
Bank has established a leadership role and is better placed than other donors
to offer assistance in the major institutional reforms under the project. The
Bank's  involvement  would  help  initiate   and  accelerate  the  process  of
implementing the policy and institutional reforms and provide for the required
continuity, while the investment financing would enhance the operating
efficiency of the existing surface transport system, as well as to help the
Government to carry out its ambitious sector expenditure program.
C.   Project Description
3.03      The proposed project  would  consist  of the following high-priority
inveetments selected from MTOP's 1988-1992 Transport Expenditure Program:
(i) Road Investments
3.04      The road investments  would  complement  progress achieved under the
Third  Highway  Project  (2238-UR)  by   adding   to  the  programs  for  road
maintenance, rehabilitation and reconstruction on the National Highway System
and to the National Feeder Road Program, a construction program of safety
improvements on the National Highway System as well as technical assistance to
strengthen MTOP and  the  departmental  governments.    The  road component is
described in detail in Annex 2 and the location of the works shown in Maps
IBRD 20952 and 20953. Details of the technical assistance and training to be
provided are included in Annex 6.
(a)  Replacement Program
- Replacement of a 149 km section of RNI between Colonia and
Montevideo (built in 1930),including about ten structures for
bridges and overpasses;

and working visits abroad.

D.   The Action Plans
(i) Actions to be taken
3.07      The main sectoral  issues  were  identified  in discussions with the
Government during the preparation of the Bank's Transport Sector Strategy
Paper for Uruguay (Report  No.  6747-UR  of  May  11,  1987).   Based upon the
findings, the Government established a series of actions directed at resolving
the sector's major issues, and a set of related yearly operational targets to
be achieved during  the  1989-1995  period.    The  actions, incorporated into
several action plans covering policy and institutional reforms, as detailed in
Annex 1 and summarized below, were confirmed during negotiations.
3.08      At a sector-wide level,  reforms  would  aim at strengthening MTOP's
management and planning capabilities and at ensuring sufficient cost recovery
from highway users. The main actions contemplated to attain these objectives
include:
(a)  development of an Intermodal Transportation Planning Model;
(b)  strengthening  of   MTOP's   and   the   modal   agencies'  planning
capabilities   through    introducing    the    necessary   planning
methodologies and providing tools and staff training; and
(c)  review of present level of road user-charges to ensure cost recovery
and development of a mechanism to adjust charges when needed.
3.09      In the roads subsector, reforms  would  aim at:  strengthening DNV's
planning capacity; upgrading engineering capacity and quality control in DNV
and  the  departments;  improving  operation   of  the  highway  network;  and
strengthening road maintenance planning  and  organization.   The main actions
contemplated to meet these objectives would be:
(a)  preparation of a rolling  Five-Year  Road Investment and Maintenance
Plan;
(b)  revision  of  primary,  secondary  and  tertiary  networks  based on
present traffic levels and function of roads;
(c)  upgrading of engineering  and  quality  controls through adoption of
modern methods of design, adequate laboratory facilities and field
soil exploration;
(d)  establishment of DNV's  Traffic  Department  with responsibility for
vehicle axle weight distribution  regulations,  setting up of a data
base for traffic  accidents,  improvements  in  design standards and
deployment of traffic control devices; and



- 17 -
(e) setting-up of systems for the preparation of rolling multi-annual
programs for routine and periodic maintenance of the National Road
network and of annual rural roads maintenance programs for the
departments.
3.10      In the ports subsector,  reforms  would pursue: improving efficiency
in provision of port services; improving investment planning, operational
efficiency and equipment maintenance management, all of these at ANP level;
improving port financial management systems; and, at MTOP level, promoting a
wider participation of the private sector in selected port-related activities
and reorganizing dredging  activities.    These  objectives  would be attained
through the following proposed actions:
(a) review of the role of each agency in the ports subsector, including
dredging services, and promoting wider participation of private
sector in provision of selected services;
(b) review of ANP's organizational structure, functions and staff
levels;
(c)  upgrading of ANP's marketing and commercial research capabilities;
(d) establishment of ANPrs rolling multi-annual investment budget;
(e) reorganization of the Montevideo port in specialized port areas,
including revision of working methods and staff allocation;
(f) introduction of an efficiency indicators system in ANP, including
adoption of productivity-based compensation systems for workers;
(g) establishment of an efficient equipment management system in ANP;
and
(h) review of existing accounting and costing procedures, including
establishment of the internal audit function and of cost-based
tariffs  to  ensure  cost  recovery   at  ANP;  establishment  of  a
commercially-oriented general and cost accounting system for tourist
and fishing ports, within DNH.
(ii) Targets
3.11      In the case of the ports subsector, the implementation of the Action
Plan would be also monitored against the following operational performance
targets which were confirmed during loan negotiations:

E. Costs and Financing
3.12      The total  project  cost  including  contingencies  is  estimated at
US$257.1 million with a foreign exchange component of US$136.0 million or 53Z
(Table on page 20 and Tables 4.1 and 4.2). Local costs, amounting to US$121.1
million, include US$48.8 million in taxes, mainly value added tax (21Z on net
values of goods, services and works, and custom duties averaging 20Z on CIF
cost for equipment). These costs reflect November 1988 prices.
3.13      The total cost of the road component, including technical assistance
for MTOP, is estimated at US$192.4 million, of which US$97.3 million is the
foreign exchange cost and US$95.0 million the local cost component. The taxes
and duties included in the local costs amount to US$34.7 million. Cost
estimates are based on: (a) detailed engineering for the Highway and Bridge
Replacement Program: (b) detailed engineering for the identified subprojects
of the Highway and  Bridge  Rehabilitation  Program; (c) estimated average per
kilometer costs of routine and periodic maintenance of departmental gravel
roads; (d) prevailing consultant fees (US$4,000 per man/month for expatriate
individual consultants (mostly Argentinian ar.d Brazilian) and US$2,000 to
4,000 per man/month for local), and manpower estimates for the technical
assistance for the Institutional Development Program; and (e) updated costs
for computer hardware and software and other ancillary equipment to support
the technical assistance programs.
3.14      The  total   cost   of   the   port   component   is   estimated  at
US$64.7 million, of which US$38.6 million is the foreign exchange cost and
US$26.1 million the local cost, including taxes and duties amounting to
US$14.1 million. This port component will finance the ANP and the DNH port
programs, whose partial cost estimates are as follows: (i) the total cost of
the ANP program is US$34.9 million of which US$20.5 million is the foreign
exchange cost and US$14.4 the local cost, including taxes and duties amounting
to US$7.8 mi_lion; and (ii) the total cost of the DNH program is estimated at



- 19 -
US$29.8 million, with a foreign exchange cost at US$18.1 million and US$11.7
million of local costs including US$6.2 million of taxes and duties. Cost
estimates are based on: (a) preliminary engineering for civil works on all
items except for port works at Punta del Este for which final design has been
completed; (b) average in.ernational prices for equipment; (c) prevailing
consultant  fees  and  manpower   estimates  for  the  professional  services,
including supervision of construction  and training (additional information is
provided in Annex 6).   Capital  dredging  at  La  Paloma included in the port
component would be executed utilizing dredging equipment currently owned and
operated by ANP and/or DNH; the estimated cost of this item is based on
international prices with an estimated foreign exchange cost of about 702 to
cover depreciation, maintenance, irsurance and fuel.
3.15      Cost estimates include physical contingencies of 10Z for civil works
and all other items except for civil works of the ANP port program, for which
15? is provided. Price contingencies were calculated on projected changes in
local and international prices during the project period e3timated at 3? p.a.
for 1988 through 1990, and 3,5% p.a. for 1991 onward.
3.16      The project would be financed from the proposed Bank loan of US$80.8
million (or about 31? of project costs), a loan from the untied facility of
Export-Import Bank of Japan (Eximbank) of US$73.9 million (or about 29Z of
project costs), and contributions _from the Government and ANP amounting to
US$93.2 million and US$9.5 million, respectively (or about 40? of project
costs). The financing plan is detailed in a table on page 20 and Tables 4.3
through 4.5. Taxes and duties for the highway component and DNE ports program
will be financed by the Government and ANP will finance those corresponding to
its port program.   Government  financing  required  for  the project would be
covered through the  flational  Budget  and  it  shculd  include US$2.0 million
corresponding to the compensation to ANP for the costs of La Paloma dredging.
ANP's financing would be covered from its internally generated funds. During
negotiations it was agreed that the Government will include in its National
Budget the necessary counterpart funds required for the project. Eximbank
would participate in the financing of all project components. For the Bank-
Eximbank  cofinancing  operation,   joint   financing  arrangements  would  be
followed: the cost of all works, goods and services to be procured would be
shared in agreed proportions (Table 4.3); contracts for all works, goods and
services would be placed in accordance with procurement procedures acceptable
to the Bank (para. 4.08); and bidding would be open to all parties who are
eligible under Bank guidelines. The fulfillment of conditions of disbursement
under the Eximbank loan agreement and the signing of a colenders' agreement
between the Bank and Eximbank would be conditions for loan effectiveness.
3.17.     The Government through MTOP would be  the Borrower of the Bank loan.
The Government would on-lend funds from the proposed loan to ANP for the
implementation of the Montevideo port component, including the institutional
developmunt program. Terms and conditions of the subsidiary loan agreement
would be substantially the same as those of the Bank loan. The effectiveness
of a subsidiary loan agreement satisfactory to the Bank, would be a condition
for loan effectiveness.

F.   Economic Justification
3.18      The  project  would  finance  part  of  the  Government's 1988-1992
Transport Investment Program and contribute towards introducing major policy
and structural reforms as well as institutional strengthening in the sector
through the  implementation  of  the  various  Action  Plans.   The project's
benefits would therefore go well beyond the returns on specific individual
investments.   The  Action  Plans  ara'  expected  to  improve the management,
administration and operation of both the entire sector and its subsectors.
The economic rate of return (ERR) estimates for the major civil work
components were based on consultants' and DNV' studies included in the
Project File. The assumptions and detailed calculations are summarized in
Annex 7 and the Montevideo port traffic analysis presented in Annex 8. The
overall ERR for the major civil works and equipment components would be about
282.
3.19      For the road  subsector,  the  construction  of  Route  1, on a new
alignment would greatly reduce vehicle operating costs, lower the existing
high accident rate, generate vehicle and passenger time savings and reduce
the need for frequent and costly overlay, rehabilitation and maintenance of
the existing road. These benefits are expected to yield an economic rate of
return of about 262.   The  rehabilitation  of  routes  6  and 7 and of other
selected high priority national roads and bridges would yield benefits mostly
in the form of reduced vehicle operating costs, reduced maintenance costs and
prolonged economic lives of those  facilities.   A detailed economic study of
Routes 6 and 7 shows that these roads would yield ERRs of 22Z and 18Z
respectively and are therefore justifiable fo; inclusion in the project.
Prior to the inclusion of any other roads for rehabilitation in the project
during  implementation,  their  ERRs   would   be  updated,  using  the  same
methodology as for Routes 6 and 7, to ensure that ERRs exceed 122. The
routine and periodic maintenance program for the departmental roads would
reduce transport costs on these roads as well as prolong their useful lives.
The ERRs for the roads to be included during the first year of the program
have becn est.mated  on  the  basis  of  these  benefits  and the routine and
periodic maintenance costs. Only roads with ERRs e:..ceeding 122 are included
in the first year program. The roads to be included in subsequent years of
the program would be analyzed with the same method and roads with ERRs
greater than 122 included.
3.20      For the ports  subsector,  the  investments  in the Montevideo port
would improve port productivity, thus reducing cargo handling costs and ship
waiting time and prolong the lives of ports equipment. The construction of
the closing dike would reduce maintenance dredging costs and provide an
additional 300 hectares of sheltered space for the ship repair industry and
other commercial activities and yield an ERR of about 762 from the reduction
of maintenance dredging alone.   the provision of additional electrical power
supply would produce benefits in the form of reduced ship-waiting times,
savings in labor costs and prevention of traffic diversion to other ports, to
yield an ERR of about  502.    Paving  of  the general cargo terminal back-up
area, demolition of old sheds and construction of new ones, purchase of
container handling equipment, construction and equipment of maintenance
workshops and construction of foundation for cranes would combine to yield an
ERR of 462. The construction of additional wharves at the Punta del Este
port would increase the marina capacity and yield an ERR of about 432 from
benefits derived from port charges for the added mooring spaces, and the
value added to additional economic activity generated by the additional boats



- 22 -
and tourists. The construction of a new breakwater and dredging of the wharf
areas and the expansion of the water supply system would permit the La Paloma
port to become Uruguay's main  fishing  port with benefits accruing from port
charges, increased fishing activity, reduced boat waiting times and savings
in transport costs to the Montevideo fishing port: the enlargement and
modernization of the Piriapolis port would permit the town to develop the
full potential of its tourism industry and complement that of nearby Punta
del Este. The benefits would include added direct revenue resulting from the
growth in boat traffic, in particular those accruing from increases in
repairs and maintenance of boats and seasonal mouring of boats. Finally.
benefits would also result from the added incremental revenue from visitors,
residing either in hotels or private residences, and from those passing
through Piriapolis on their say to and from Punta del Este and the interior.
These benefits are expected to yield an ERR of about 22Z.
G.   Environmental Aspects
3.21      No adverse environmental impact is expected as a result of works to
be carried out under the roads component of the project. Regarding the ports
.omponent, the Montevideo Master Plan Study (December, 1987) included a
detailed environmental analysis that concluded that the project would not
affect existing pollution levels in the Montevideo Bay. With regard to the
La Paloma port, a hydraulic study carried out by the University of Montevideo
indicated that the construction of the proposed breakwater and deepening of
the ba.in would not cause  any  adverse  environmental impact, but would help
expand adjacent beaches.      In  addition.  during negotiations the Government
agreed at all times to take or  cause  to be taken all necessarv measures and
precautions to ensure ttat the  operation  and maintenance of the ser-ond fish
processing plant, and related  activities,  are  in  full comipliance with its
appli( able environmental protection and pollution contrnl rulls,  regulations,
standards and practices, which at nu time would be less strict than those
presently in effect in the Borrower's territory or stri(ter than those
applicable to the existing fish processing plant at La Paloma.
H.   Project Risks
3.22      The project would  cover  highways,  the  Montevideo port and small
craft ports and involve several  agencies.        There  is, therefore, a risk of
slow project implementation. The project, however, has been designed to take
this into account: a Project Coordinating Unit would be established within
MTOP to coordinate and monitor implementation l ogress. Also, the project's
main thrust would be the far-rerching sector policy and institutional
reforms. Some risk exists that the next Government may falter in carrying
out the agreed reforms.   In  order  to  reduce that possibility, the project
arrangements provide for joint annual reviews to enable the Bank to continue
to have an effective dialogue for the implementation of the reforms.
IV. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION DETAILS
A. Subproject Selection
4.01      Most of the components included  in  the project are fully defined,
i.e. civil works in Routes 1, 6 and 7, all port components and technical
assistance and training.      However,  the  rehabilitation  and maintenance of
additional priority highways and bridges, and works to be carried out after



- 23 -
the first year under the departmental roads maintenance program would be
defined during project implementation and agreed with the Bank at the semi-
annual consultation meetings.   In  order  to  be declared eligible under the
loan, civil works still to be defined would have to be technically sound and
economically justified. Annex 7 explains in detail the economic evaluation
criteria to be used for the inclusion of these works. Works that do not
qualify under the preceding eligibility criteria would be replaced by new
ones as agreed upon by the Bank and tho Borrower.
B. Project Execution
4.02      The proposed project would be  completed  in about six years by the
Government through MTOP and ANP.   DNV. within MTOP, would be responsible for
the  implementation  of  the   road   component  including  the  departmental
maintenance  program  for  which   specific  procedures  have  been  designed
(Annex 9). A draft of the model agreement between MTOP and participating
departments for the execution of the maintenance program was reviewed at
negotiations. Signing of the agreement with at least 50Z of the departments
would be a condition for disbursements of the roads and civil works
component.  Two copies of each  agreement  would  be  sent to the Bank.  DNH,
also within MTOP, would be responsible for the La Paloma, Punta del Este and
Piriapolis  port  components  and  ANP  for  the  Montevideo  port component.
Submission  to  the  Bank  of  satisfactory  soil  investigations  and  final
engineering for the closing dike included in the Montevideo port component
would be a condition for disbursements for the construction of the dike. The
exnansion of the water supply system  at  La Paloma port would be carried out
by MTOP with the technical supervision of Obras Sanitarias del Estado (OSE).
Upon completion of these facilities, they would be operated and maintained by
OSE, under an agreement satisfactory to the Bank. The responsibility for the
implementation of the institutional development program would rely on DNV,
DNH and ANP; however, this program would be closely monitored by MTOP.
Dredging at La Paloma, would be carried out by force account by ANP, on
behalf of DNH. The Project Implementation Schedule is given in Chart II.
4.03      The execution of  the  project  would  be coordinated and monitored
through a Project Coordination Unit (PCU) to be established within the MTOP.
The PCU would be headed by a full-time professional, satisfactory to the
Bank, backed-up by full time professionals from all the agencies cor,cerned.
The PCU would act also as direct liaison with the Bank. The establisl;ment of
the PCU and the appointment of its full-time head would be a condition for
loan effectiveness.    During  negotiations  agreement  was  reached with the
Government on the functions and responsibilities of the PCU.
4.04      The  highway  replacement  and  rehabilitation  programs  would  be
contracted and supervised by DNV strengthened under the Action Plan included
in the project; the maintenance program of departmental roads would be
carried out by the departments assisted by MTOP and DNV (Annex 9). DNH has
adequate capabilities to carry out the procurement and supervision of
construction of  items  under  its  responsibility.    ANP  also has adequate
experience to carry out the procurement of its project items; however, since
responsibilities and functions are not well defined within ANP, a Technical
Unit (TU) would be established to be in charge of procurement and to retain
consultant services. ANP would retain consultants f3r the supervision of
construction. During negotiations, agreement was reached with the Government
on the responsibilities of the TU within ANP, and on the retention of
supervision consultants by ANIP.    The  establishment  of  the  TU would be a



- 24 -
condition of disbursement of loan proceeds for works and equipment under the
responsibility of ANP.
4.05      The  training  component   for   ANP   would  be  administered  and
implemented with the support of a technical assistance institution. ANP
would establish a Training Steering Committee (TSC), strengthen the existing
Training Center and enter into a contract agreement with a technical
assistance institution satisfactory to the Bank, no later than June 30,
1989. During negotiations agreement was reached on the composition of the
TSC and the Training Center and on the timing for contract signing with a
technical assistance institution. The establishment of the TSC would be a
condition of disbursement of loan proceeds for the ANP training component.
4.06      MTOP, DNV, DNH and  ANP  would  be resj3nsible for the coordination
supervision  and  implementation  of   their  specific  technical  assistance
components. During negotiations, agreement was reached with the respective
agencies on the timing and scope of the technical assistance (Annex 6).
4.07      Implementation of the Actions Plans  would be the responsibility of
the Governmeit through the Economic and Financial Advisory Unit, within MTOP,
with the partic.ipation of all the agencies concerned. During negotiations,
agreement was reached on  maintaining  this  unit.   The strengthening of its
monitoring cepabilities would be a condition of effectiveness of the Loan.
4.08      It  was  agreed  at  appraisal  that,  within  six  months  of  the
completion of the proposed project the Government, with the executing
agencies, would prepare a Project Completion Report (PCR) to assess the
success in the implementation of the project and the related Action Plans,
including the achievement of financial, operational and other institutional
targets and a general evaluation on the lessons learned from implementation
experience.
C.   Procurement and Consultancy Services
(i) General
4.09      All civil works and goods included in the project would be procured
following International Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures and Local
Competitive Bidding (LCB) procedures acceptable to the Bank, in accordance
,-ith Bank guidelines and subject to limitations in contract values for LCB
set forth in paras. 4.10 and 4.11 below. All ICB contracts would be subject
to the Bank's prior review of the procurement documentation. In addition,
the first five LCB contracts for goods and for works, respectively, will also
be  subject  to  prior   Bank   review   of  the  procurement  documentation.
Consultants would be retained in accordance with Bank Guidelines for the Use
of Consultants.
(ii) Civil Works
4.10      The proposed Bank  loan  would  help  finance  road civil works for
about US$188.3 million and port civil works for about US$48.7 million. Of
this total, ICB procedures would be followed for works totaling about
US$148.7 million, LCB procedures for works totaling about US$26.7 million,
and works totaling about US$61.1 million (departmental road maintenance)
would be executed by force account. The minimum cost of individual contracts
or of packages of smaller contracts to be tendered following ICB procedures



- 25 -
has been set at US$2.0 million.    The  experience of the last five years has
shown that 17 ICB tenders (five financed by IBRD and 12 by IDB) for similar
and even higher amounts failed to interest foreign contractors. Procurement
of works under LCB procedures, acceptable to the Bank, would allow the
participation of foreign bidders, either on their own or associated on a
voluntary basis with local contractors. Force account would also be used for
the execution of the La Paloma port dredging (US$2.0 million), the latter to
be financed exclusively by the Government and not shown in Table 4.0.
(iii) Goods
4.11      The proposed  Bank  loan  would  also  help  finance acquisition of
computer hardware and software and miscellaneous engineering equipment and
vehicles for highways totaling US$2.1 million and for ports equipment
totaling US$9.7 million. Of this total, ICB procedures would be used for
about US$7.6 million, LCB for about US$1.4 million, Limited International
Bidding (LIB) for about US$0.9 million and Local Shopping (LS) for about
US$1.9 million. The minimum base cost plus taxes of items to be tendered
following ICB procedures has been set at US$0.3 million and -he minimum for
LCB procedures at US$0.05 million; items costing less than US$0.05 million
could be procured by LS.   LIB  procedures in accordance with Bank guidelines
would be followed for computer hardware and software. Awards of contracts
for imported goods would be evaluated on a CIF basis, taking into account the
freight costs quoted freely by each bidder; no restrictions would be impospd
with regard to imported materials  and  goods.   Bid evaluation for equipment
procured under ICB procedures  would  allow  a  margin of preference to local
bidders equivalent to 152 of CIF costs of competitive imports, or the
relevant pre-railing custom duties, whichever is lower.
4.12      During negotiations, agreements  was  reached  with  regards to the
procurement arrangements described above (paras. 4.09 through 4.11). Table
4.0 provides a summary of procurement arrangements for items to be financed
under the proposed Bank and Eximbank loans including physical and price
contingencies:
D.  Disbursements
4.13      Disbursements of the Bank loan would be made against:
(a) 292 of total expenditures of civil works under the highway program,
except for the gravel roads maintenance program;
(b) 302 of MTOP contribution to partially finance the gravel roads
program;
(c) 292 of total expenditures for civil works under the Montevideo port
component;
(d) 312 of total expenditures for civil works under the La Paloma, Punta
del Este and Piriapolis port components;
(e) 60Z of the foreign exchange expenditures of imported goods; 60Z of
the ex-factory cost of locally-manufactured goods; and 502 of local
expenditures for imported goods locally procured;
(f) 502 of foreign and local expenditures for professional services; and
(g) 602 of total expenditures for all training components.

4.14      Bank funds are expected  to  be disbursed over a six-and-a-half-year
period, in line with the standard profile for transport projects in the
Region. The estimated schedule of disbursements is given in Table 4.6.
Retroactive financing in respect of payments made for expenditures prior to
the signature of the Bank loan but after April 30, 1988, in an aggregate
amount not exceeding US$2.0 million equivalent, would be made in respect to
works  at  Punta  del   Este   and   professional  services  included  in  the
Institutional Development Program and for supervision of construction. All
disbursement related matters and retroactive financing were discussed and
agreed upon at negotiations.
E.   Special Account and Auditing
4.15      In order to maintain an  adequate  flow of funds to finance eligible
project expenditures, with a minimum of administrative delay, the Government
would establish a special account in its Central Bank, under terms and
conditions satisfactory to the Bank.    The  Bank would, upon the Government's
request, deposit an aggregate amount equivalent to a maximum of US$8.0 million
from the loan account into the Bank Special Account, sufficient to cover about
four months of project expenditures.   Records of the Special Account proceeds
and outlays would be available for review by Bank supervision missions and
subject to  annual  audit.    Disbursements  would  be  made  on  the basis of
certified  Statement  of  Expenditures   for   local  expenditures  where  the
contractual values are less than the equivalent of US$300,000 and for foreign
expenditures where contractual values are less than the equivalent of
US$50,000; the corresponding supporting  documentation  would be verified on a
routine  basis  during   the   supervision   process.        During  negotiations
confirmation was obtained of the foregoing arrangements.
4.16      The Tribunal de Cuentas  (TC)  perfo.ms  the external audit for ANP.
TC has recently improved the quality of the auditing work by hiring qualified
accountants.   It  also  performs  commercial  audits  at  the  request of the
clients. During negotiations, agreement was reached on the external auditing
of all entities involved in the proposed project by TC in accordance with
appropriate auditing principles and on the forwarding of the annual audit
reports to the Bank not later than six months after the end of each fiscal
year. The audit will include the Special Account. Should TC not be able to
carry out the audit and issue report on time, private independent auditors,
acceptable to the Bank, would be appointed to complete the task.
F. Project Monitoring and Semi-Annual Consultations
4.17      With re.gard  to  project  monitoring,  in  addition  to the progress
reports to be pr-pared by the Government, the Bank would: (a) annually,
starting  in  mid-1989,  review  the   progress   of  the  Action  Plans,  the
implementation of the agreed recommendations emanating from the technical
assistance components, the progress and results obtained from the training
program and the achievement of port operational targets and financial
covenants; (b) hold semi-annual  consultation  meetings with MTOP (around June
and November) to review progress in the implementation of the roads and ports
component, and to review budget and programs for the upcoming year (further
details for the roads component are provided in Annex 9). During negotiations
these matters were discussed and agreed with the Government and ANP.



- 28 -
V. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
5.01      During negotiations, agreement was  reached  with the Government and
ANP on the following:
(a) to complete the road user charges revenue study by October 31, 1989;
to discuss it with the Bank; and to make the necessary adjustments
to the level of charges by  December  31,  1909, so as to reflect at
least the short-term marginal cost of maintaining and administering
the network (para. 2.07);
(b) to provide necessary foreign exchange and local counterpart funds
required for the project (paras. 2.23(a) and 3.161;
(c) that ANP would restructure its tariffs based on the new costing
system by March 30, 1990 (para. 2.23(b)j;
(d) ANP would not incur any debt, in addition to the proposed loan
unless its net operating revenues remain at least 1.5 times the
annual debt service charges (para. 2.23(c)];
(e) ANP to achieve an operating ratio of 87 in 1990, 84 in 1991, 82 in
1992, 80 in 1993, 80 in 1994, and 79 in 1995 (para. 2.23(d)];
(f) ANP to achieve financial rates of return on revalued fixed assets of
2.0 in 1990, 2.6 in 1991, 2.9 in 1992, 3.3 in 1993, 3.7 In 1994 and
4.0 in 1995 (para. 2.23 (e)];
(g)  to  inform  the  Bank  on  each  updating  of  the  Transport Sector
Expenditure Program (para. 2.30);
(h) that the Government would confirm the start-up of operations of a
second fish processing plant in La Paloma, as a condition for
disbursement of the La Paloma port component (para. 3.06);
(i) operational performance targets for ANP (para. 3.11);
(j) that the Government at all times would take or cause to take all
necessary measures and precautions to ensure full compliance with
its environmental protection and pollution control rules in the
operation and maintenance of the second fish processing plant at La
Paloma, which at no time should be less strict than those presently
in effect in the Borrower's territory or stricter than those
applicable to the existing fish processing plant (para. 3.21);
(k) that signing of the agreements for the departmental maintenance
program between MTOP and at least 50Z of the departments would be a
condition for disbursement of the gravel roads maintenance program
(para. 4.02);
(1) submission to the Bank of soil investigations and final engineering
of the closing dike would be a condition for disbursement for the
construction of the dike for the Montevideo port (para. 4.02);
(m) functions and responsibilities of the PCU (para. 4.03);



- 29 -
(n) responsibilities of the TU within ANP; retention of supervision
consultants by ANP for the Montevideo port component (para. 4.04);
(o) the establishment of the steering committee and TU would be a
condition of disbursements for works, goods and training under ANP's
responsibility (para. 4.04), while the establishment of TSC is a
condition of disbursement of ANP's training component (para. 4.05);
(p) ANP to strengthen the existing training center and enter into a
contract  agreement   with   one   or   more   technical  assistance
institutions, satisfactory to the Bank, for the administration,
coordination and implementation of ANP's training program, no later
than June 30, 1989 (para. 4.05);
(q) the timing and scope of the technical assistance (para. 4.06);
(r)  proposed   implementation   details,   including   procurement   and
disbursement matters,  use  of  the  special  account,  auditing and
project monitoring arrangements  (para.  4.12,  4.15, 4.16, 4.17 and
4.18); and
(s) details of semi-annual consultations (para. 4.18).
5.02      Conditions for loan effectiveness would be:
(a) fulfillment of conditions of disbursement under Eximbank agreement
and the signing of a colenders' agreement between the Bank and
Eximbank (para. 3.16);
(b   effectiveness of a  subsidiary  loan  agreement  satisfactory to the
Bank, between the Government and ANP, for the implementation of the
Montevideo port component (para. 3.17); and
(c) establishment of the PCU (para. 4.03).
(d) the strengthening of the monitoring capabilities of the Economic and
Financial Advisory Unit (para. 4.07);
5.03      Subject to the above  the  project  provides  a suitabie basis for a
Bank loan of US$80.8 million.   The  terms  would be 15 years with a five-year
grace period.
