URUGUAY
TRANSPORT PROJECT I
The Roads Subsector
A. Introduction
(i) Highway Administration
1.        For administrative  purposes,  the  highway  network  in  Uruguay is
classified into national and  departmental  networks.   Vialidad, the National
Highways  Directorate  (DNV)  within   MTOP,   is  responsible  for  planning,
constructing, maintaining and operating the national highway network through
its headquarters in Montevideo and nine regional offices. Outside the direct
control of DNV is the departmental network, which is controlled by the
municipal administration of each department (Intendencia) for construction and
maintenance.
(ii) The National Network
2.        The national highway network has 9,660 km of roads of which 6,795 km
are paved, 2,835 have gravel surface and 30 km earth surface (Table 1). The
network is divided into three systems: major arterials (3,800 km), collectors
(3,800 km), and local roads (2,060 km).
3.        The extent of the National Highway Network is adequate.  The general
condition of the network is less satisfactory than the one evaluated by MTOP
in Table 1 (562 in good, 412 in fair, and 3Z in poor condition). The
principal problems are the age of most pavements and their seriously
deteriorated condition. The design standards for many roads constructed prior
to 1950. are outdated and inadequate to provide safe operating characteristics
for tne present traffic volumes and higher speeds. The lack of an efficient
maintenance management system has contributed -ign1ficantly to the prvement
deterioration.
(iii) The Departmental Network
4.        The departmental networks  have  66,235  km  of low standard, poorly
maintained roads and streets of which 1,961 km are paved surfaces, 24,496 km
are gravel surfaces and 39,778 km are earth surfaces. Of this total, 13,580
km are urban and suburban streets and, therefore, the actual rural roads
network is of 52,655 km (296 km paved, 19,208 gravel and 33,151 earth). Table
1 shows the poor condition of this network (16Z good, 28Z fair and 56? poor)
stressing the need for remedial  actions.    The network needs some additional
feeder roads in areas with high agricultural potential.
5.        The  current  World  Bank   project   (Loan  2238-UR)  provides  for
rehabilitation and reconstruction of about 6,300 km (US$15 million) of
existing departmental roads from all of the departments. At present 5,328 km
have been completed and 973 km are under construction.



- 50 -
ANNEX 2
Page 2 of 8
(iv) The National Highway Directorate (DNV)
6.        DNV is organized  into  four  directorates  (Chart 1): Financing and
Administration, Investments and Works, Road Maintenance, and Engineering.
Each one of these directorates is subdivided into two to four departments
according to specific functions. Supervision of construction and execution of
maintenance works are carried out by the regional offices. All directors, and
all chiefs of departments and regional offices are well-trained professionals
with several years of  experience.    The depletion of competent professionals
that DNV suffered in the 1960s and 1970s has been overcome in recent years by
expanded hiring of young professionals who are receiving intensive training
and are rapidly climbing into mid-management positions. DNV's road planning,
engineering, work supervision, maintenance and network operating capacity
would be further strengthened under the proposed project.
(v) Municipal Road Administration (Departments)
7.        The capacity and capabilities  of  the  18 departments vary, but all
have an infrastructure division responsible for public buildings and roads.
The professional level of the road administrators in the departments has
improved substantially during recent years and would be further enhanced, in
close collaboration with DNV, under the proposed project.
B.  PlanninglOperations/Regulations
(i) Planning and DesignrStudes
8.        Planning has  shown  marked  improvement  as  a  result  of the work
financed  in  the  World  Bank  projects  (Loans  1689-UR  and  2238-UR),  but
additional technical assistance is necessary to insure a planning capability
sufficient of developing multi-modal transport plans, establishing modal
planning priorities, and developing a planning unit capable of producing
5-year roll-over planning programs, feasibility studies and procedures and
programs to generate the data base necessary to implement the planning
process. A start has been made and the Transport Planning Unit ((UPT) of DNT
is developing the planning capacity. A national transport plan is expected to
be completed by late 1988.
9.        DNV is in the process of upgrading its capabilities and a major area
to change is  the  design  function.    Assistance  is  being  provided by the
Argentine DNV for the improvement of pavement design, and by the U.S.
Department of Transportation (DOT) for the improvement of roadway and bridge
design. DOT is also providing manuals and computer software for highway
planning,  traffic  engineering,   road   safety,  maintenance  and  equipment
management.    DNV  is  taking  steps  to  develop  in-house  capabilities for
producing the contract documents of highway design plans, standard and special
construction specifications and engineering cost estimates in lieu of using
local consultants.
(ii) Construction and Maintenance
10.       All road construction for  DNV  is  done  under unit price contracts
after competitive bidding. National contractors are required to register in



- 51 -                          ANNFX 2
Page 3 of 8
MTOP's General Register of Contractors.    DNV is planning to provide in-house
construction supervision as a result of the planned organizational expansion.
In the past unit prices for construction projects have increased at a greater
rate than prices in the general market place. Therefore, DNV has updated its
data base for cost calculation in engineering estimates.
11.       Maintenance of national highways is  performed  by DNV using its own
equipment, personnel  and  materials.    Maintenance  is  carried  out by nine
regional offices sub-divided  into  26  districts.    Each  regional office is
allocated its own equipment and has a field workshop for routine equipment
maintenance. A central workshop in Zolon, and private garages perform major
equipment repairs.
12.       Routine maintenance is  generally  satisfactory, but the combination
of increasing costs, inadequate periodic maintenance and a highway network
approaching 40 years of service requires more than normal effort to maintain
an acceptable level  of  service.    Development  of  an effective maintenance
management system has a high priority for DNV at this time.
13.       The departments attempt to maintain  their networks, but the results
are uneven and often unsatisfactory.   They duplicate, on a smaller scale, the
facilities available at DNV. They are, for the most part, under-staffed, with
weak  management,  lacking  experienced   professionals,  and  suffering  from
shortages  of  equipment  and   materials,  inadequate  equipment  maintenance
facilities  and  minimal  funding.       Design,  construction  and  maintenance
practices are nct standardized bw'.-ee  the  depaiuuteau awu Iur tne most part
they do not apply DNV's national network standards.
14.       Cooperation between DNV and  the  departments  has improved with the
MTOP decision to focus on the rehabilitation and maintenance of the highway
networks in Uruguay. The current Bank loan (Loan 2238-UR), which initiated
the National Feeder Roads Program, has been revised to implement the MTOP
commitment to maintenance and expand the Traffic Safety component. DNV is
putting more emphasis on technical assistance and is proposing to expand the
maintenance training function within DNV.
(iii) Labor
15.       DNV  currently  has  3,324  employees  working  in  the headquarters
office, the central workshop and the nine regional offices. The work force
has three major classifications defined as follows: (a) budgeted personnel who
are permanent, participate in the promotional  ladder and are eligible for all
benefits; (b) permanent personnel who  enter  the  service at a high level, do
not participate in the promotional  ladder  and are eligible for all benefits;
and (c) temporary and contract personnel, working in the regions, who do not
participate in the promotional ladder and may be released if work is slack.
Unskilled labor may not be transferred between regions, but all other
personnel are eligible  to  fill  openings  in  lieu  of  being released.  The
general job classification of the personnel is as follows:
(a)AaA - Professionals (engineers)
(b)Ab - Administrative Personnel
(c;AcC - Technicians
(d)AcB - Skilled and Unskilled Labor
(e)Ad - Service Personnel

16.       The distribution of DNV's  personnel by job classification indicates
that 93Z of the staff is primarily temporary labor6rs that are not on the
promotional ladder and receive no  benefits.    These people rely on the labor
unions to provide assistance in the job market and represent them in labor
disputes. This situation is particularly counter-productive in the operation
of the central and regional  workshops.   Overstaffing in the central workshop
has been reduced substantially in the last year, from a total workforce of 500
to 390 at  present  of which  28  are  professionals,  260  mechanics and 102
administrative and aux-iliary personcl, stare-keepers nnt drivers.
(iv) Construction Industry
17.       The construction industry in Uruguay is well organized.  In order to
qualify for participation in bidding for highway projects, contractors are
required to register in MTOP's    General  Register of contractors, where each
firm is classified according to field of specialization, financial capacity,
availability  of  equipment,   staffing,   annual   work   capacity  and  past
performance. At present, twenty-five contractors are registered for highway
and bridge projects with five classified as first level contractors, and five
as second level. Additionally, two contractors are classified exclusively for
bridge projects.
(v) Vehicle Weight, Size and Enforcement
18.       The 1975 regulations for vehicle  size and operation were revised in
1984 and again, in 1986. Vehicle size has been increased to cover the larger
international truck and trailer units (maximum length between axles of 15
meters) from Brazil and Argentina and weight limits have been raised
accordingly from 37 tons to 42 tons to be in line with the Argentine limits of
45 tons and the Brazilian limits of 40 tons. Enforcement is carried out at
permanent scale locations at major access points to the country and by teams
using portable scales to check movements on the country network. When
overloading is discovered the violator is expected to reduce his load to the



- 53 -
ANNEX 2
Page 5 of 8
legal limit before continuing and is fined an amount of Unidades Sectoriales
de Transporte (U.S.T.) which are presently valued at 960 new pesos, and are
calculated as a function of the excess in tons, moved over a distance (kms) of
the road system. The fine can vary from 2 U.S.T. units for 5 tons of excess
weight moved from ze,.o to 25 km to the maximum fine of 850 U.S.T. units for
moving 70 tons for 275 km or more.
(vi) Training and Technical Assistance
19.       DNV has taken a positive  aggressive attitude toward the improvement
of training and technical assistance (TA) for DNV and the departments. Three
outside institutions have been able to provide necessary assistance to DNV in
this area. The World Bank, through the Third Highway Project has provided
funds for TA and also provided training for MTOP personnel in the use of the
Highway Design Model (HDM), a Bank developed computerized design and planning
tool.   The  Argentine  DNV,  through  a  country  agreement,  has completed a
preliminary pavement inventory of the Uruguay highway system using two
deflectographs. They have trained DNV in the use anl operation of the
machines >a.d in the analysis of the results to determine pavement structural
performance. The Bank is financing two of these machines for DNV under the
current loan. The DOT, through a country agreement has completed a report for
DNV on pavement design using basaltic aggregate and has completed an analysis
of this type of material.   In  addition,  DOT  has agreed to provide DNV with
computer software covering all of the highway plant functiorns, training in the
use an.- '.     t   v.. -r the  Mr        a. a                             __ -_  _!  .
The Bank is  financing  the  necessary  hardware, basically micro-computers to
establish the system.
20.       DNV is in the process  of  reorganization in order to streamline the
operation of the highway plant and the need for training the departments is a
major consideration.    Training  is  currently  a  section  in  the equipment
maintenance department, but DNV plans to upgrade the training section to the
department level and attach it to the soils and materials investigation
division. As a training department the scope of the program will be expanded
to provide training and technical assistance across the range of functions
necessary to the operation of the highway plant.
C. Demand/Supply
21.       The Transport Planning  Unit  (UTP)  of  DNT  has  been conducting a
manual classification traffic counting program on the National Highway Network
as part of the Institutional Strengthening of MTOP and DNV. This program has
been in effect since 1983 and has counted traffic on 25 national roads at 99
count stations for periods ranging from 16 hours to 24 hours per day.
Analysis has been slow and UTP provided annual average traffic data in 1986
for the 1984 count program.   Implementation of the computerized systems being
made available from DOT should help speed up the processing. In addition to
the classification count program, UTP also conducted coverage counts on routes
from the major arterial, collector and local road system to provide input for
the economic evaluation of specific  sub-projects.   UTP also conducted origin
and destination surveys to provide input for the economic evaluations.



- 54 -                         ANNEX 2
Page 6 of 8
22.       While these  programs  demonstrate  a  great  improvement in traffic
survey and count coverage in Uruguay, more technical assistance is necessary.
Although UTP is currently responsible for these programs, DNV is setting up a
traffic division or department capable of providing for traffic operations,
engineering, safety,  surveys  and  studies.    A  traffic  engineer  has been
appointed and is implementing planning procedures and performing some initial
surveys to develop a data base of accidents prior to the beginning of the
technical assistance program. The department has absolutely no expertise or
experience in network operation, traffic engineering principles, or highway
safety and will need training and technical assistance.
D. Pavement Performance
23.       Analysis  of  the  design   and  behaviour  of  pavement  structures
indicates that the pavement structures on much of the existing networks are
inadequate, over-aged and under-designed for the new axle load regulations.
DNV is aware of the problem and has started several programs to inventory the
conditions of the networks, measure the structural capacities, and standardize
design practices. The Third Highway Project is providing funds for technical
assistance to support these  programs.    The technical assistance provided by
the Argentine DNV in the analysis of the design and behaviour of pavement
structures has resulted in DNV establishing new monitoring and design
procedures, procurement of deflectograph equipment, and preparation of network
inventories.
z4.       rarr or tne probiem W;i  paiveent perfoance ir Uruguay  s .c1ated
to environmental conditions and constructior materials such as aggregate and
asphalt. In 1985 two sections, RN12 and RN23 of the Third Highway Sector
Project which had been in service for one year had pavement failures. These
two links are located in an area that has considerable ground water in the
sub-base and base. As part of a country-to-country agreement signed in 1984,
DNV requested technical assistance from the DOT in assessing the problem. The
results indicated that the pavement failures were primarily due to the use of
basaltic  aggregates  in  the  bituminous   concrete  mix.    In  addition  to
investigating these two failed sections, DOT tested seven other locations and
determined that the probable contributing factors to pavement failure are a
combination of:   poor  quality  granites  in  the  mix; improper construction
techniques where ground water is found in the base and surfacing; inadequate
structural design; and importation of poor grade of crude oil and the lack of
quality control at the refinery.
E. Equipment Management
25.       Equipment management is weak  in  both  DNV and the departments, but
DNV is taking positive action to strengthen it. Equipment maintenance suffers
from a shortage of trained personnel, labor union influence, poor management,
operational deficiencies anc inadequate controls. A large part of the problem
is the tendency to provide and staff duplicate facilities in an area with
limited resources. The DNV central workshop performs only 30Z of the needed



- 55 -                         ANNEX 2
Page 7 of 8
repair work, with 702 going  to  garages  in  the   private sector, and DNV is
budgetting more funds to districts for use in private garages for small repair
and maintenance jobs. The use of private garages has created a need in DNV
for inspectors to monitor quality  control.   The departments also use private
garages to perform work that their workshops cannot handle, but many times the
necessary expertise is not available in the departments and equipment must be
moved to Montevideo making the monitoring of quality control much more
difficult.
26.       Before DNV can  implement  an  effective Equipment Management System
the Labor Union problem must  be  resolved  and the question of duplication of
services between private and government repair facilities and between parallel
DNV  and  departments  repair  facilities  addressed.    Implementation  of  a
computerized  Equipment  Management  System  should   result  in  a  level  of
standardization that would make better use of existing resources and reduce
the need for the duplication of facilities.
27.       DNV has identified some areas of the operation that can be improved.
Equipment management data would be used to implement a maintenance production
line that would schedule equipment for routine servicing based on usage hours
and establish production norms.    Training  of  heavy equipment mechanics for
diesel, hydraulics, transmissions and other specialities in short supply would
be added to the agenda for both DNV and Intendencia mechanics. A roadside
repair and servicing unit would be established to reduce the need for taking
equipment from the job site to a district or central shop for minor breakdowns
aUld LUut.LUC beLV..L.
F. The Proposed Project
28.       The  highway  component  of   the   proposed  project  is  aimed  at
strengthening road management capability and MTOP's transport man gement
capability,  and  at  providing   investments  urgently  required  to  improve
transport operations along specific road sections that are deteriorating
quickly and/or  are  unable  to  carry  present  traffic  demand.   A detailed
description of the project is as follows:
(a) Physical Investments:
(i) Replacement Program
-  replacement  of  a  149  km  section  of  Route  1 between Colonia and
Montevideo (built in 1930)  on  a  new  horizontal and vertical alignment
(according to modern design standards)  including about 10 structures for
bridges and overpasses;
(ii) Rehabilitation and Maintenance Program
- rehabilitation of a 15 km section of Route e in a suburban area north
of Montevideo, including one bridge, one rai..road overpass, about 14
improved ad-grade intersections, and partial widening to four lanes;
- rehabilitation of a 51 km section of Route 7 north of Montevideo,
including about 6 bridges;



- 56 -                          ANNEX 2
Page 8 of 8
- rehabilitation and maintenance of additional priority highway sections
and bridges.
(iii) Department Maintenance Program
- a first year program for routine maintenance of a maximum of 10,000 km
of selected gravel roads in participating departments and periodic
maintenance of about 2,000 km of those roads;
- further annual routine maintenance programs over the project period
for a maximum of 10,000 km of selected gravel roads in participating
departments and annual periodic maintenance programs on about 2,000 km of
gravel roads, both subject to annual review to determine whether the
selected sections are technically and economically justified;
(iv) Institutional Strengthening of MTOP
- improvement of MTOP's capabilities in transportation planning and
management,  including  preparation   of   a multi-modal  transportation
computer model, review of road-user charges, provision of a training
program for transport management and participation in international
conferences and working visits abroad.
(v) Institutional Strengthening of DNV and Participating Departments
-  improvement  of  DNV's  and  the  department's capabilities in highway
planning, engineering, traffic management, soils and materials testing,
traffic management and road maintenance and equipment management;
- preparation of computer models for planning, traffic management, and
road maintenance and equipment management;
- acquisition of computer hardware and software, laboratory equipment,
surveying equipment, weighing scales and light vehicles;
- participation in international conferences and working visits abroad.
(b) Action Plan for Institutional Reforms
k9.       MTOP's Action Plan for roads includes all those measures intended at
strengthening sector policies and increasing institutional efficiency and is
supported by the technical assistance and training provided for in the
strengthening programs described above.    Further  details of the Action Plan
are provided in Annex 1.

URUGUAY
TRANSPORT PROJECT I
The Ports Subsector
A. The Port System
1.        The port system in Uruguay  plays  an important role in handling the
country's foreign trade and transit cargo from neighbouring countries.
Cabotage, however, is relatively  small.    The system includes facilities for
passenger services to/from Argentina, services the foreign fishing fleets
operating in  the  South  Atlantic  and  the  domestic  fishing  fleet.   Port
facilities to serve the small craft fleet of recreational boats are also part
of the port system.
2.        Port development reflects the  physical constraints imposed by water
depths available along the Atlantic coastline, the Rio de la Plata basin and
the Uruguay river, as well as the demographic distribution of the country.
Montevideo, the only deep-water port, is located in the bay of Montevideo
facing the Rio de la Plata basin; Colonia the second commercial port in terms
of overall traffic (cargo and passengers) is also in the Rio de la Plata basin
at a location limited by shallow waters (5 m); the other two commercial river
ports, namely Nueva Palmira y Fray Bentos are located along the Rio Uruguay
with depth restrictions imposed by that of the waterways (6.5m). The other
commercial river ports of Paysandu y Salto have practically ceased to operate
because of depth constraints or because of new bridge connections with
Argentina.
3.        La Paloma on the Atlantic  coast, designated to become the country's
main fishing port, serves three fish processing plants located there and its
expansion is planned to enable it to cope with the growth of the fishing
industry. Piriapolis is a small port serving another fish processing plant.
However, being located in the country's most important turistic area, plans to
develop Priapolis as a small craft port, second only to Punta del Este, are
well advanced.
4.        The port system includes a  number  of small craft harbors extending
from the Uruguay river to  the  Atlantic  coast.   Some of them offer marinas,
launching ramps and full small craft services, while others are just natural
havens providing mooring/landing facilities.
B. Port Facilities
5.        Montevideo is  the  only  deep-water  port  of  the  country with an
operational depth of 10 m alongside most of the 16 international traffic
berths (about 2800 m of quays). The back up area consists of about 30 ha that
accommodate 13 transit sheds and warehouses, of which 9 are obsolete and badly
located, leaving limited working and open storage areas, some of which are in
poor condition. Power supply is obsoletu and inadequate, to a degree that some
quay cranes acquired four years ago are still non operational. The above
deficiencies coupled with poor equipment maintenance, inappropriate labor



- 59 -                          ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 6
practices, and less than desirable management result in Montevideo having one
of the lowest port productivity levels in the region.
6.        The La Paloma port consists  of  a partially protected basin; it has
600 meters of quays, of which only 240 meters are operational, as dredging
alongside the rest has been postponed to avoid leaving unprotected the
facilities currently in service. All landing of fish for feed processing and
ship loading of finished products are carried out directly by the industries
installed in the port.
7.        Uruguay's network of small craft  harbors has been developed in most
cases using natural location advantages along its extensive shoreline to
provide facilities  to  pleasure  boats.    River  and  maritime  resorts have
traditionally attracted Argentine tourism helped by short distance. On the
Rio Uruguay the main small craft recreational ports are Fray Bentos, Mercedes
and Higueritas.  Along the Rio  La  Plata  in the Montevideo coastline   there
are eight small craft harbors, the main one being located at Colonia. Finally
there are three ports east of Montevideo: Buceo, a small craft harbor at
Montevideo; Piriapolis; and Punta del Este, which offers berthing and mooring
to 400 boats and inland storage for another 400.
8.         The ports in Uruguay  are  experiencing  a period of major changes.
The increasing use of containers and the trend towards larger ships are
creating opportunities and problems for Montevideo and other commercial ports.
Tf_, cpcclally s="- c.aft. tou.is=,  is icreaciang 4n -4portance creati=0
a demand for mooring  and  other  facilities.    Furthermore, the national and
international fishing fleets are increasing their activities in the South
Atlantic with an increasing demand for shore installations.
C. Port Institutional Framework
9.        The principal agencies involved in ports include:  MTOP, through DNH
and DNT; ANP; the Ministry of Labor and Soc'al Security, through the National
Administration for Stevedore Services (ANSE); the Ministry of Economy and
Finance, through Customs, foreign trade regulations and naval industries; the
Ministry of Defense and the Navy, through the National Coastguard (PNN); and
the Ministry of Agriculture, through the National Institute for Fishing
(INAPE). In addition, the recently-created Ministry of Tourism is expected to
be involved in promotion of small craft ports and harbors.
10.        ANP  is  responsible  for  the  administration and operation of all
commercial  ports;  its  current   functions  include  cargo-handling  (except
stevedoring which is carried out by ANSE), towage, dredging and port
construction. In addition to the above, it operates the same old merchant
vessels and ship repair facilities for all its floating fleet (vessels
dredgers and barges, tugboats and other floating craft) and is responsible for
granting licences for port services, within the port of Montevideo. Most of
ANP's staff is assigned to Montevideo, the rest being distributed among the
port administrations at Fray Bentos, Nueva  Palmira  and Colonia.   The agency
is legally autonomous but is required to seek government approval for tariff
increases, investments and the annual  budget.   The multiple non port-related
functions assigned to ANP, coupled with an obsolete overstaffed organization,
make it necessary to undertake an in-d pth reorganization, seeking to



- 60 -                          ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 6
eliminate those functions that can be performed by others and establishing or
upgrading  those  considered  essential   to   transform   ANP  into  a  fully
comiercially  oriented   enterprise.        The   project   would  support  such
reorganization.
11.       DNH, is responsible  for  constructing  and  operating all the small
craft harbors and  the  fishing  port  of  La  Paloma.    DNH  owns a fleet of
dredgers, barges, tugs and other equipment for the maintenance of navigable
river waterways and buoy markings, and is also engaged in the provision of
ferry and other river crossing  services.    DNH's role would be revised under
the proposed project, simultaneously with the reorganization of ANP, to define
functions and responsibilities, avoid duplication, achieve coordination, and
promote a wider participation of the private sector in the development and
operation of small craft ports and harbors.
D. Port Operations
12.       In  Montevideo  port,  inappropriate  port  operation  practices and
poorequipment  maintenance,  coupled   with   lack   of  coordination  between
stevedoring  and  on-shore  operations,  are   the   main  cause  of  the  low
productivity of the port.
13.       In order to measure port  productivity at Montevideo port statistics
were analyzed and compared with those of other ports of similar size and
traffic level (Table 1 of this annex).   Productivity was measuLed it eii  uf
the following standard performance indicators: (a) Berth Performance Index
(BPI) or average rate based upon total time ships occupy berths; (b) Cargo
Performance Index (CPI) or average rate based upon net time ships are engaged
in loading and unloading cargo; and (c) Gang Performance Index (GPI) or
average rate based upon CPI and the average number of ship hatches worked
simultaneously per ship; this index is expressed in terms of tonJgang/hour.In
general, the comparison of performance indicators precludes adjustments for
bringing all ports into an absolute common basis, since there may be disparity
in port working hours, size of shipments, weather conditions, etc. To isolate
the effect of these factors, the Cargo/Berth (CIB) ratio which measures time
actually spent in loading and discharging ships as a proportion of total time
that ships occupy berths, was utilized.
14.       The comparison of  performance  indicators  (Table 1 of this Annex),
leads to the following conclusions:
(a) Cargo Performance Index (CPI) at Montevideo is about 60? of that of
other ports; this indicates that port workers are not able to handle
cargo at reasonable rates due to the lack of coordination between
stevedoring and on-shore cargc handling;
(b)  Gang  Performance  Index  (GPI)   at  Montevideo  show  that  hourly
productivity is about 622 of the average rates of developing
countries due to: (i) inappropriate cargo-handling methods (a large
percentage of cargo is handled through direct delivery to trucks);
(ii)  lack  of  incentives   to   increase  efficiency  in  on-shore
operations (payments to these workers are not related to cargo
handled); and (iii) abscence of regular training for port workers to
bring them up to date with modern cargo handling technologies;



- 61 -                          ANNEX 3
Page 4 of 6
(c)  the comparison  of  C/B  ratios  indicate  that  idle  time of ships
alongside berths is considerably longer  than in other ports.  While
in Montevideo ships are idle for about 60Z of the total time they
occupy berths,  the  average  for  220  ports  is  only  30Z.   This
reflects, among other things, lack of port operations planning,
unsatisfactory working time controls, poor equipment maintenance,
inadequate opened and covered storage areas and vehicular port
traffic controls.
15.       The proposed project would include technical assistance and training
to reorganize port operations and improve equipment maintenance practices. It
would also be agreed with the Government that changes in labor practices and
payment systems would also need to be addressed. To measure the effect of the
proposed changes, specific efficiency indicators (Table 2 of this Annex) have
been prepared for general cargo and containers, in terms of ton/gang/hour
(GPI) and ton/ship/day (BPI). These productivity targets, however, would have
to be reviewed and refined by ANP with the support of the technical assistance
consultants to be hired under the project.
E. Physical Constraints
16.       The above mentioned low productivity  rates are also attributable to
the following physical constraints present at Montevideo port: (a) lack of an
independent access to the container terminal, (containers have to pass the
geileial L;Kgu area); (u) obsoLete sheds  (two  or three story) and narrow quay
aprons; (c) limited adequate open storage areas; (d) deficient internal
electric system to operate container and quay cranes; (e) shortage of tractors
and trailers to move containers arriving on board of general cargo ships; and
(f) obsolete maintenance facilitics.
17.       The physical investments included in  the project for Montevideo are
aimed at resolving the above deficiencies. Specifically, improvements to the
container terminal, remodeling of the general cargo and multipurpose zones and
modernizing of the power supply system and of the port repair and maintenance
facilities, have been included.
F. Towage
18.       Although there is  a  privately  owned  tugboat  which operates on a
concessionary basis, at present, tugboat services are provided mainly by ANP.
ANP's fleet consists of 7 tugboats, of which only 6 ate available for
operations (one, which was handed over by the Navy, is not appropriate for
maritime services). In order to support this fleet (and also the dredging
fleet) ANP maintains a very costly ship repair facility, despite the fact that
this type of repair services are also being provided by the private sector.
In order to eliminate from ANP all non-port related functions, which can more
efficiently be performed by others, the Government is considering the
possibility of transferring towage services in full to the private sector this
matter would be further dealt with, when redefining the role of ANP under the
proposed project.



- 62 -                          ANNEX 3
Page 5 of 6
G. Dredging
19.       Uruguay's  maintenance  dredging   needs  include  navigation  river
channels along the Uruguay river, shallow-water river ports, and the maritime
access channel and port basin at Montevideo. Currently, maintenance dredging
is carried out by two agencies, namely ANP and DNH. ANP owns six dredgers, of
which only three are fully operational; two of them are hopper dredgers and
are currently used to maintain the Montevideo access channel and outer basin
and the third one is a small stationary suction dredger not suitable for
Montevideo. DNH owns seven dredgers of which at least three should be
scrapped, the remainirg four are stationary cutter suction and grab dredgers
and are used  for  maintaining  all  ports  but  Montevideo.   This equipment,
however, is not suitable for maintaining the river channels. Details of the
dredging fleet are given in Table 3 of this Annex.
20.       ANP's and DNH's combined dredging capabilities should, in principle,
be sufficient to cope with maintenance dredging requirements at a national
level.    Consequently,  the  Government  should  consider  unifying  dredging
activities under one single authority to optimize the use of available
equipment. The transfering of the existing repair and maintenance facilities
to the private sector should also be pursuee. Under the project,technical
assistance is being provided to: (i) more precisely define maintenance
dredging requirements, particularly at shallow water river ports, navigation
channels and small craft ports; (ii) develop an integrated dredging plan;
(iii) develop an organizational scheme to unify dredging operations; and (iv)
develop a dredging equipment maintenance plan and define the most efficient
way to implement it.
H. The Proposed Project
21.       The port component of the proposed project is aimed at resolving the
most  important  institutional  issues  facing   the  port  subsector  and  at
providing, at tne same time, those investments urgently required to eliminate
bottlenecks and improve port infrastructure to increase operating efficiency.
A detailed description of the project components is as follows:
(i)   Physical Investments:
(a) At Mont.evideo
-  construction  of  a  shallow-water    losing  dike,  at  the
northern end of the port basin to red ce siltation caused by
the tidal flow from the Montevideo bay to the port area;
-  construction of en independent  access road and gate complex
at the container terminal, including a trailer parking area;
-  remodeling the general  cargo  area, by demolishing obsolete
warehouses, and repaving the back up areas, including
-  remodeling  pier  8  to   convert  it  into  a multipurpose
terminal, by demolishing four obsolete two story transit
sheds and constructing two new ones; paving the quay apron,
open  storage  and   back   up  areas,  including  upgrading
utilities and reinforcing quay cranes foundation; and



- 63 -                          ANNEX 3
Page 6 of 6
-  acquisition  of  yard   tractors   and  trailers  to  handle
container traffic in other port areas, outside the container
terminal.
(b) At La Paloma
- construction of the western breakwater to provide for the
expansion of the fishing port;
- dredging works to expand the access channel and the port
basin; and
-  civil works and equipment to expand the water supply system.
(c) At Punta del Este
- construction of 340 m of additional small craft berths.
(d) At Piriapolis
- extension of existing breakwater; and
-  construction   of   berthing   and   boat-repair  facilities
including land reclamation and paving.
(e) Port Institutional Strengthening
- technical assistance to support the projects institutional
reforms, including:   studies  to  redefine  the role of the
subsector agencies; consultant services to upgrade ANP's
organizational,   administrative,   planning,   operational,
maintenance   and   financial   capabilities;   studies   to
reorganize  dredging   administration   and  operation;  and
consultant  services   to   upgrade   DNH's  accounting  and
financial systems;
- training of ANP personnel at all levels, including courses
and seminars in Uruguay and abroad; scholarships in port
administration  and   planning;   and   work  visits  abroad
including    attendance    to    specialized   international
conferences; and
- training equipment and materials and computer hardware and
software for the implementation of the new information
systems.
(ii) Action Plan for Institutional Reforms:
22.       HTOP's Action Plan for ports includes all those measures intended at
redifining sector policies and increasing institutional efficiency and is
supported by the technical assistance and training provided for in the
strengthening programs described above.    Further  details of the Action Plan
are provided in Annex 1.

URJGUAY
TRANSPORT PROJECT I
Financial Aspects
I.   ANP
A.   Introduction
1.        ANP  is  an  autonomous   state-owned  enterprise  responsible  for
provision or cargo handling and marine services, construction and maintenance
works, and granting of licenses for port services within the port of
Montevideo. In addition, it administers the ports of Fray Bentos, Nueva
Palmira and Colonia. ANP operates within the framework of Law 5,495 (1916)
as modified by Decree 8,988 (1933) and Law 11,037 (1948), with a Board of
five full-time directors, all named by the President of the Republic. The
Board  is   assisted   by   a   General   Manager,   three   Deputy  Managers
(Administration, Technical and Commercial) and 9 Directorates with advisory
functions. Port financial management is the responsibility of the head of
the Finance Division, with lower hierarchy than Deputy Managers in the
organizational chart, but also reporting directly to the General Manager.
Although the agency is legally autonomous, it is required to seek Government
approval for tarirf increases, Investments and the annual budget.
B.   Costs and Tariffs
2.        ANP's costing system suffers from numerous deficiencies: costing is
limited to just a few operations, such as workshops and civil works, and
based only on direct labor and material costs; costs are not grouped by cost
center or center of responsibility; and, the pricing procedures to assign
costs to parts and materials are not uniform among the various stores.
Furthermore, rno standard cost concept is used to permit management to measure
actual vis-a-vis standard costs.   In  order to eliminate these deficiencies,
the financial consultants hired under the previous port project (Loan
1798-UR) designed a new port costing system by cost center. The new costing
system is being implemented an is expected to be fully operational by end-
188. In order to ensure that the new system works satisfactorily, the
consultants would be invited back to examine it, under the proposed project.
In addition, they will be asked to develop the marginal cost concept, which
was not initially introduced, since this is the first step for the
establishment of cost-based tariffs.
3.        Because of the lack  of  reliable  cost data, ANP's present tariffs
bear no relationship with the cost of services rendered. Tariffs are quoted
on an ad-valorem basis and import tariffs more than double export tariffs.
In addition, ANP is, by regulation, allowed to grant price discounts to
exporters on an at random basis.   A recent analysis of costs and revenues of
imports and exports showed the following results:



- 68 -                         ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 9
Import         Export
Tariff per ton (USS          24.90         11.14
Total ccst per ton           23.80          23.80
(excluding interest)
Estimated long-run           14.00          14.00
marginal cost
Indeed, present export tariffs are not even covering long-run marginal costs.
Finally, numerous tariff exemptions exist for fishing vessels; at Montevideo
port tariffs for fishing activities are grossly understated and at La Paloma,
no charges are levied on  any  vessel.    All  the above disparities would be
eliminated through the establishment of a cost-based tariff structure, which
would be pursued under the proposed project.
C.   Budgeting, Accounting and Auditing
4.        Until recently, ANP's budgeting  system  was  deficient and did not
fully comply with legal requirements.   The budget was prepared merely on the
basis of past year's figures, without taking into account future planning
and/or adjustments in real programming of works. In addition, inflation was
not taken into account in determining costs. Under the previous port project
(Loan i796-Urk)ossl.        wieizs  - -  .d  tc  desn a s n rphAnsive budaetary
control system based upon planning, setting of objectives and programming of
work. The consultants have completed their task and the new budgeting system
is  now  being   introduced.       Furthermore,   based  on  the  consultants'
recommendations, ANP has created a Consultative Commission within its
administration to successfully operate the budgetary system. This Commission
is expected to meet regularly between April and July of each year to discuss,
and to ultimately finalize the budget proposals prepared by a Technical
Committee representing each department of ANP. The Technical Committee would
prepare  budgets  for  capital  and  recurrent  expenditures.    The  capital
expenditure budget would be prepared for five years while the current
expenditure budget for one year.   Once  finalized, the budgets would be sent
for approval, first, to ANP's Board of Director, then, to OPP and finally to
the President of the Republic.    ANP  should  ensure  that the new system is
fully instituted.
5.        ANP's accounting system was  also  revised by the financial consul-
tants hiLed under the previous Bank loan.  The new system, which is currently
being instituted eliminates most of the deficiencies of the previous one:
data processing is computerized; the new classification of accounts is cost-
oriented; financial reports will be the responsibility of just one reliable
source, rather than the previous two or more sources; financial and cost
accounting will be fully integrated; and the budgetary and accounting systems
will be properly linked to allow for budgetary control.
6.        ANP's internal audit  needs  strengthening.    It  does  not play a
dynamic role in internal control of port operations and administration and
does not act as a useful liaison between the external auditors and ANP.
During the implementation of the proposed project necessary technical
assistance will be provided in  this  field.    The external auditors a.e the



- 69 -                          ANNEX 4
Page 3 of 9
Tribunal de Cuentas (TC), who perform a legal audit. The TC has qualified
auditors from local universities and perform the audit work fairly well. A
review of their 1985 audit report indicates that TC has improved the quality
of its work and has demonstrated its willingness to perform a commercial
audit in addition to the legal audit, if it is specifically requested by the
client.  In light of  these  developments,  the appointment of TC as external
auditors would  not  be  objectionable  to  the  Bank.    During appraisal an
understanding was reached with TC  that  they  will audit ANP's accounts, the
Special Account and the SOEs.   The  TC  agreed  to issue the long form audit
report and the Management  Letter  and  submit  them  to  the Bank within six
months of the end of  fiscal  year.    If,  due  to heavy workload, the TC is
unable to produce the audit report  within  the agreed time frame, they would
agree to the hiring of other commercial independent auditors for the job. An
appropriate covenant is included in the loan documents.
D.   Past Financial Performance
(a) Operating Results
7.        Table 1 of this Annex presents  ANP's income statements for 1982 to
1986, which are summarized as follows:

8.        During the 1970s, ANP's financial  performance was good.  Beginning
1980, the financial situation started deteriorating and, by 1984, the net
surpluses turned into net  losses.    The  working and operating ratios first
showed improvement in 1983 over 1982 (65 and 82 in 1983 vs. 72 and 89 in
1982), but thereafter deteriorated to reach  93 and 109 respectively in 1986.
Even though  the  financial  results  of  port  operations recorded operating
surpluses up to 1985, the amount of these surpluses was declining. The
operating revenues of N$ 1.3 billion in 1982 turned into an operating loss of
N$ 0.7 billion in 1986.   The  main reasons fcr ANP's deteriorating financial
situation  were:  (i)  increasing   staff   coEts;  (ii)  declining  traffic.
particularly the high revenue-earning import  traffic; (iii) low tariffs; and
(iv) weak cost-control mechanisms.



- 70 -                          ANNEX 4
Page 4 of 9
9.        In order to improve  its  financial  position,  ANP should focus on
improvement in operating efficiently and quality of service, reducing costs
particularly the staff costs and increasing tariffs selectively. ANP's
administration is also involved in activities which would be advantageously
performed by others. For example, the dry dock operations have no direct
relationship with port operations; and dredging, at a national level, is
performed by both ANP and DNH, a situation which has led to duplication of
facilities and equipment and to lack of proper coordination for the dredging
of interior  and  coastal  ports.    A  complete  reorganization  of  ANP is,
therefore, required.   Consideration  should  also be given to private sector
participation in the provision of selected services. The project will focus
on strengthening the organization and management of ANP through the provision
of the necessary technical assistance.
(b) Cash Flow
10.       Because of its satisfactory financial performance during the 1970s,
ANP accumulated a cash surplus of about US$50 million by the early 1980s.
However, since the Government was experiencing serious economic and financial
difficulties at that time, it instructed ANP, in 1983, to transfer US$22
million if its surplus  to  the  Treasury.    In  addition, ANP was forced to
finance 50Z of the construction of the container terminal and the fishing
berths, amountin" to US$10 million, which the Government had initially agreed
to finance but failed to do  so.   These two important cash outflows, coupled
1-  Shc .t,!Ltv- -;.^"                                 -ka-a   wsls.4e,1 1begryn 4,n fhe ee-rl,-
LAY   L*C              -                                             -    _.        
1980s (para. 8) aggravated ANP's cash position substantially. Furthermore,
ANP's weak management failed to adopt remedial measures in a timely manner,
and neither costs were controlled no tariffs adjusted in line with the
declining traffic and revenues. ANP's total revenues were not sufficient to
cover cash deficits during  1984  to  1986.    For  Montevideo port, the 1986
revenues were not even sufficient to cover working expenses. Consequently,
ANP had to resort to short and long-term loans to cover its financing gap.
The gradual erosion of the peso also contributed to increase current
liabilities related to external suppliers, further aggravating its cash
position.
(c) Balance Sheets
11.       ANP's balance sheets  for  1982  to  1986  (Table  2 of this Annex)
indicate that the net fixed assets increased, in nominal values from N$ 6.4
billion in 1982 to N$ 47.7 billion ill 1986 (7452 net increase) but actually
decreased from N$ 102.6 billion to N$ 82.3 billion (202 decrease) in constant
1987 pesos. This substantial increase in the nominal value is due to the
revaluation of fixed assets and the devaluation of the peso, and the decrease
in constant values is due to  almost  no new investment and retirement of old
assets.  Similarly,  current  assets  rose  from  N$ 596  million  in 1982 to
N$ 1,847 million in 1986  iA4  nominal  value  (310Z increase) but in constant
terms declined,  mainly  due  to  declining  receivables  and  cash balances.
Current liabilities increased from N$ 383 million to N$ 2,895 million for the
same period (about 756Z increase).    The  current and liquid ratios worsened
from 1.6 and 1.4 in 1982 to 0.6 and 0.5 respectively in 1986. This confirms
ANP's inability to pay off its current debts. The most serious problem was
that the total long-term debts increased from N$ 545 million in 1982 to
N$ 6,657 million in 1986--a net increase of over 110O. The debt equity



- 71 -                          ANNEX 4
Page 5 of 9
ratio has been satisfactory in this  period  but  mainly due to the effect of
revaluation of fixed assets.
E.   Future Financial Perfarmance
(a) Financial Objective of ANP
12.       ANP should always remain  a financially self-sufficient enterprise.
ANP should aim to overcome present financial difficulties and generate
revenues sufficient to cover all operating expenses, debt service charges,
and a contribution to future investments for renewal or expansion of port
facilities. In order to achieve this objective it would be necessary to
restructure ANP. Based on this restructuring a financial forecast has been
prepared and is discussed in the following paragraphs.
(b) Financial Forecast
13.       The financial tables comprising  forecast Income Statements (Tables
3 and 6), Fund Flow Statement (Tables 4 and 7) and Balance Sheets (Tables 5
and 8) are attached to this Annex.    Each table was prepared on the basis of
'with restructuring of ANP' and 'without  restructuring of ANP'.  The results
clearly  indicate  that without   such   restructuring,  ANP  cannot  become
financially viable.   The  Income  Statement  under the restructuring program
(Table 3) demonstrates that  the  working  and operating ratios would improve
from 86 and 103 in 1987 to a healthy 61 and 79 respectively in 1995.  In real
terms, net operating revenues would  improve  from  an N$ 0.3 billion loss in
1986 to an N$ 2.8 billion profit in 1995. Starting in 1990 ANP's revenues,
including  depreciation,  will  cover   all   operating  expenses,  all  dabt
obligations and a satisfactory contribution toward future investmentE or
replacement  of  assets  and  expansion  of  facilities.    In  addition, the
restructuring would enable ANP, starting in 1993, to transfer funds to the
Government Treasury (Rentas Generales) from its annual net surplus (Table 4).
However, in the initial years of the implementation of the project, ANP would
need a Government subsidy totaling N$6.2 billion (US$20 million equivalent)
in the 1988-1989 period.  The financial  rate of return on revalued net fixed
assets would improve substantially from -0.62 in 1987 to 42 in 1995. The
financial targets such as the operating ratio, rate of return on net fixed
assets, tariff restructuring and Government financial contribution are part
of the conditionality for the proposed project.
14.       Under the 'with  restructuring'  case,  ANP's  debt service charges
would be gradually reduced to about US$3.7 million a year in 1995 as against
US$18.0 million in  1988.    No  additional  local  or  foreign loans will be
secured by ANP between 1991 and 1995. A debt limitation covenant is included
in the loan documents.
15.       The forecast Balance Sheets (Table 5) indicate that the debt equity
ratio would remain, throughout the forecast period, at a very satisfactory
level. The current ratio would improve from a poor 0.4 in 1987 to a modest
1.8 in 1995. Long-term loans will gradually reduce from a high of N$ 10.3
billion in 1987 to N$ 6.5 billion in 1995.
16.       Based on the above, it  is  evident  that if ANP is restructured it
can produce impressive results.    If,  however,  it is not restructured, the
financial situation will deteriorate further, the Government financial



- 72 -                          ANNEX 4
Page 6 of 9
obligations will be much  higher  and  ANP  will  not remain competitive with
neighboring ports.  The following is  a summary of comparative results of the
'with' and 'without' restructuring program:
With           Without

Under the 'without' situation  ANP  would  continue to have puor productivity
performance and excess  staff  costs  of  over  N$  2  billion  a  year.  The
financial rate of return on net fixed  assets  would only oe 1.42 in 1995 and
the Government would end  up  paying  a  subsidy  of  N$  11.4 billion to ANP
(contrary  to  receiving  cash   dividends  under  the  'with  restructuring'
forecast).   In  addition,  with  the  improvements  in  operating practices,
reorganization and institutional  strengthening,  ANP  can become technically
productive. commercially competitive and financially a viable concern.
*c) Sensitivity Analysis
17.       A sensitivity analysis was carried out to assess the impact of some
risk elements as follows:
(a) Absence of Government subsidy; and
(b) Delay in manpower reductions.
If the Government's financial assistance of US$20 million is not provided ANP
as assumed in the forecast, ANP will have to seek additional high cost loans
from local sources.   This  will  increase  the overall debt obligations. ANP
would be in dat,per of getting caught into the vicious circle of borrowing
funds in a  continuing  manner  only  to  pay  past  debts.   It is therefore
advantageous to both the Government and ANP that the former provides the
required financial subsidy.   Concerning  manpower,  if  ANP failed to reduce
staff costs as assumed, by 1991 the operating ratio would be about 112?
instead of 842 as shown in the first forecast, and there would be a cash
deficit of about US$37 million during the forecast period. Furthermore, net
losses would continue  until  1994.    This  would  definitely impose a heavy
additional financial burden on the public finances.
II. SMALL CRAFT AND FISHING PORTS
18.       DNH is responsible for  operating  and  maintaining the small craft
ports at Punta del Este, Piriapolis and the fishing port at La Paloma, all of
which are part of the project.



- 73 -                       ANNEX 4
Page 7 of 9
A.   Punta del Este Port
(a) Past and Present Financial Performance
19.       DNH does not have  an  elaborate  accounting  or costing system for
this port. The revenue and cost figures are shown in global terms and it is
difficult to carry out a detail cost analysis or evaluation of finances.
With the help of consultants, attempts were made to prepare Income Statements
and the Funds Flow Statements for 1983 to 1987 (Tables 9 and 10). The
results indicate that Punta del Este has become a highly profitable port
since 1986 (Table 9). In addition. it has benefitted from the fact that the
Government has been paying all investments and debt service charges.
(b) Financial Forecast
20.       A financial forecast for the  Punta  del Este Port was prepared for
1988-2000 period. All assumptions used for this forecast are recorded in
Attachment 1 of this Annex.   Tue  Income Statement and Funds Flow Statements
are shown in Tables 9 and 10.    The net revenues, after covering total costs
and interest charges, show a continuous rise from N$ 78.5 million in 1990
(before completion of the proposed project) to N$ 198.7 million in 1995 and
to NS 242.4 million  in  the  year  2000.    The working and operating ratios
improve from 32 to 52 in 1988 to  22  and 37 in the year 2000 (Table 9).  The
cash balance will increase from N$ 147.6 million in 1988 to 2,659.6 million
in tne year 2000.  This porr is presently being used at full capacity and the
demand, as per consultants, is 50Z higher than its present capacity.
Although tariffs in real terms have been increased at 102 during 1991, 1995
and 1999 in the forecast, the port could afford to increase them even more
without any impact on the demand.   Punta del Este is therefore a financially
viable port and the total cost recovery through revenues is not a concern at
all.
B.   La Paloma Port
21.       La Paloma is a  commercial  and  fishing  port.  DNH maintains this
port and dredges the channel as and when necessary. Like Punta del Este, DNH
does not maintain proper records which can provide details of revenues and
costs by each activity to ensure achievement of financial cost recovery.
22.       This port does not generate any  revenues.  MTOP has been absorbing
total capital and operating costs through the Government budget. No tariffs
are charged to either foreign or local ships for the use of port facilities.
Even the private fishing industry located at the port, ASTRA, is presently
using about two hectares of land free of cost from the Government. In
addition, in August 1985, the Government also introduced incentives on fish
exports (112 on filet and blocks) and fuel price subsidy of 33? (tax
exemption) to benefit ASTRA.   However,  the  Government should ensure that a
port cost recovery program is established, through adequate tariffs, from
1992 onwards, when the proposed project will be completed.
23.       Based on investment needs,  the  financing  plan agreed between the
Bank and the Government, traffic forecasts prepared by DNH and consultants,
and the major assumptions noted in the attachment 1, forecast Income
Statements and Funds Flow Statement (Table 11) for La Paloma were prepared
for the years 1988 to 2000.    In  this  forecast, focus was centered in cost



- 74 -                          ANNEX 4
Page 8 of 9
recovery of port operations through revenues, to ultimately achieve the
financial self-sufficiency of the port. It should be noted that tariffs were
set at levels which will not discourage the clients from using the port. In
addition, ASTRA's financial situation was analyzed to determine its capacity
to start paying port dues. The results are shown below:

After incurring net losses in 84/85  and  85/86, ASTRA generated a net profit

profits (US$ 4.2 million in  1987  to  US$  4.6  million in 1989).  ASTRA is,
therefore. in a comfortable Dosition  to  start  pavinR  toward the use of La
Paloma Port.  The  port, however  neeCs at least another factory of a similar
size as ASTRA'E so  that  the  port  operat4ng  costs  can  be shared by them
without any unoue financial burden on them.
24.       With the  cost  recovery  program  for  La  Paloma  port the Income
Statements (Table 11) indicate that the estimated working and operating
ratios of 200 and 877 respectively (beginning of port charges at a small
scale) in 1991 will substantially improve to 18 and 94 in the year 2000
(Table 11). The net operating losses will decrease gradually from N$ 143.5
millior in 1988 to N$ 12.7  million  in  2000.   The La Paloma port will also
have a net cash balance of N$ 70.4 million by 2001, which should be
sufficient for the size or operations the port is expected to have (Table 9).
By the year 1993, the port will be able to generate funds to cover all of the
working costs (excluding depreciation, which is also a source of funds); by
1994 the revenues will cover the working costs and interest on debts; and by
1998 the revenues will also cover some of the debt payments. The Government
is willing to provide annual financial contributions until the port can stand
on its own.   From  1990  to  2001  the  total  financial contribution of the
Government is e timated to be N$ 3.4 billion (about US$10.0 million).  Around
the year 2000 t   port should become financially self-sufficient.
25.       The Bank reached agreement during negotiations that disbursement of
loan proceeds for the project's  proposed  investments  in this port will not
materialize until another  fishing  plant  started-up  its  operation next to
ASTRA.
26.       Through the technical assistance  under  the project, the La Paloma
port would be assisted in establishing a commercially based accounting
system, a billing system, a costing system and a cost based tariff structure.



- 75 -                          ANNEX 4
Page 9 of 9
C. Piriapolis
27.       Since the demand for a tourism  port  is higher than Punta del Este
can accommodate, the Government decided to develop Piriapolis so that excess
traffic from Punta del Este can be diverted to Piriapolis. In addition to
the port's normal revenues, two additional revenue s,urces are expected to
provide substantial income to this port. These are:
li) leasing to private concessionaires for commercial development of
about 4,000 m2 of land owned bi DNH within the port boundary,
starting from 1994, at US$40 per m over 30 years; and
(ii) the  transfer  of  about  US$2.6  'iiiior  by  the  Intendencia  de
Maidonado to the port (net presenit valv.e discounted at 6Z) over 15
years, starting in 1996.   These  fundf  will be generated from the
appreciation in values of land and properties (real estate) located
next to the port.   The  total  area  is estimated to be 20,000 m2,
which will be exploited by the Intendencia and will be developed by
the private sector.
28.       Based on  the  investment  plan  and  major  assumptions  used, the
financial forecast was  prepared.    The  Income  Statement  (Table 10) shows
improvement in Working and Operating ratios from 1081 and 2690 to 13 and 57
in  year  2000.    The  port  is  expected  to  generate  sufficient revenues
f4-IsiA4,a 4toea t4i gnu  14i  nof para.  971 to  cover all operating costs
including depreciation.  After 1995 the port revenues will begin to cover all
operating costs and interest  charges.    The  Fund Flow Statement (Table 11)
indicates that together with Governmint financial contribution (which would
be for capital cost and debt payment o, investments on the breakwater and the
dike financed by IBRD and Eximbank), the Port has potential to become
financially viable. Because of debt service charges on the breakwater and
the dike are to be paid in 17 yearf, the Government Contribution will
continue until year 2001. The total Gwvernment contribution during 1987-2003
would be about N$2.5 billion which is less than the capital cost of the
breakwater and the dike. But if the fund flow to the Port from land and
property value appreciation does not materialize, then the Government
contribution will be substantially increased from N$2.5 billion to N$3.7
billion -- a 502 increase.   From year 2004 no Government contribution should
be required by Piriapolis as it is expected to be fully financially self-
sufficient. During these years the port would also build up a good cash
balance resulting in N$67 million in 2004.
29.       The Government agreed to a plan  of action which will ensure a cost
recovery program through  user  charges.    Through  the technical assistance
under the project, this port will  also  be  provided with help in setting up
accounting, costing, tariff and billing systems.

A. Majior Assumptions and Notes for the
Financial Forecast for ANP
1.        The major assumptions used in the financial forecast were:
(i)  Currency Value and Exchange Rate
2.        The forecast  was  prepared  in  March  1988  constant  N$, and the
exchange rate used was US$1=316 N$. The forecast period is 1987 to 1995.
(ii) Traffic, Tariff and Revenues
3.        The traffic projections were made by the consultants INTECSA during
the preparation of the Master Plan for  ANP.   This forecast uses 19a5 as the
base year, and from then onwards the same growth rate assumed by INTECSA.
4.        Tariff increases were assumed only  for berthing and port dues (for
use of port) at  10?  annually  during  1988-1992  period.    If ANP finds it
difficult to apply the increase in any one year then tariffs for other items
such as towing or export cargo could be increased to obtain the same overall
increase in revenues. A 12 annual increase was assumed for other services.
These increases are justified due to their present low levels. Regarding the
number of ships and the gross registered tons, no variations were anticipated
in the forecast period from  1987  onwards.    For towing, no variations were
fo.ecasted. The export and transit traffic tariffs were used based on
experience between June 1986 and March 1987 (US$11.14/ton for exports and
US$6.8/ton for transit  traffic).    Similarly,  for  use  of warehouses, the
revenue of US$1.02/ton handled was used (excluding container traffic).
Storage for containers was assumed at US$19.25/container, based on previous
records (October 1986 to  March  1987).    For  handling  of empty and loaded
containers a price of  US$34.67/container  was  used.   Tariff for imports at
US$24.39/ton was taken from a sample of June 1986 - March 1987 tariffs.
Special tariffs for fishing vessels, services to ships, use of equipment,
private berths, container handling etc. were obtained from the tariff
schedule and the average revenue during June 1986 and March 1987. For
interior ports the average revenue during October 1986 to March 1987 was
used.   The  refrigerated  storage  revenues  were  calculated  assuming full
capacity utilization of the cold storage areas from 1988.
(iii) Operating Costs
5.        The following assumptions were  used  to calculate staff reductions
at ANP.
- Staff reduction of about 232 by 1,117 (the total staff as of
October 31, 1987 was 4,860) during 1989-1993 through attrition.
Only staff over 60 years were considered for retirement so that
their income was nos drastically reduced.
- Since more than 1,117 people should be reduced, ANP could carry out
a detail study in order to determine how many more could be reduced
and by what method. The pyramid of the ages of ANP employees in
May/June 1987 was as follows:



-88-                       ANNEX 4
Attachment 1
Page 2 of 6
AGES                                     z of

46 - 55                1259             26.1?
56 - 65                1050             21.8?
Total Staff   4,686
About 50Z of the staff is over 45 years of age and 222 is between ages 56-G5.
6.        The calculation of staff cost was based on:
- reduction of 1,117 staff between 1989-1993.
- reduction of overtime cost by 252 in 1989 and 502 in 1990. On
average, overtime hours in the months of April to June 1987 were
133.000, equivalent to 834  employees.   This would represent about
2,600 workers in one year.
- Transfer of dredging eouiipment personnel (including its maintenance
crew) to some other entity, private or public (see para. 12).
- Salary increases in real term of 3? in 1990 and in 1992 to provide
incentives to the remaining employees.
- The staff costs include all other personnel costs to be incurred,
such as pension benefits, which ANP is forced to pay up to the
retirement at age 70 (beyond which Seguro Social pays).
7.        Material  and  other  costs  have  been  estimated  based  on  past
experience. Traffic growth is modest and does not warrant increases in
material costs, therefore, 1986 costs were adjusted to March 31, 1988 and
used in the forecast.
Depreciation
8.        The overall depreciation rate for fixed assets was taken as 1.6Z on
gross revalued assets. Depreciation on dredging equipment has been excluded
in the forecast as these assets should be transferred to some other entity.
Investments
9.        Total investments assumed are  only  those of the proposed project.
The forecast also includes US$0.5 million annually for replacement of fixed
assets.
Borrowing Terms and Conditions
10.       The following  borrowing  conditions  were  used  for  IBRD and JEX
(Japan) loans:

(i) IBRD - Duration of the Loan 17 years 1
- Grace period            5 years
- Interest rate           standard variable
rates (7.59Z at present)
- Service charges         0.75Z on undisbursed balance
(ii) Eximbank (Japan)
- Duration of Loan       17 years
- Grace period            2 years
- Interest rate           5.5Z
Dredging
12.       It was assumed that all  dredging  equipment and related staff will
be transferred to DNH or any other public or private entity. The new entity
will pay ANP costs annually equivalent to debt service charges on dredging
equipment. ANPf! will hire dredging services at an international price of
US$2.00/cubic meter (this may have to be increased somewhat to cover
transportation cost of dredged mud for about 5 to 7 miles away for dumping).
INTECSA consultants have estimated that ANP needs 3 million cubic meters of
dredging annually at a cost of US$6.0 million. This cost is included in the
forecast.

5.        Details are shown in INYPSA  report,  Annex 12, para. 2.3.2.1 (also
Chapter 3, paras. 3.7.3.8.2, 5.2.2). Five sets of tariffs were used:
(a)  GTR and days of stay.
(b)  Ship length and days of stay.
(c)  Monthly tariffs for fishing boats based in the port (which will not
be charged the above tariffs).
(d)  Tariffs per ton of fresh fish and anchoita unloaded.
(e)  Tariffs per loading and unloading frozen products and animal food.
These tariffs would cover total port operating costs and depreciation on
assets. All other services such as towing, cleaning and water supply would
be paid in full. Tariff increases are assumed at 10? in real terms in 1991
(first year of operation of new project) and 10? every four years thereafter.

6.       In La Paloma, no tariffs have been charged until now, therefore, the
suggested tariffs are:
(a)  Tariff MI (port use) - Same as  in  ANP (i.e. based on GRT an every
24 hours of stay or part of it US$0.9/100 GRT and day).
(b)  Tariff M2 (Moving -  General  Cargo)  - US$1.00/metership length an
day moving for fishing vessels:
- refrigerated ships                   US$600/month
- high sea boats                       US$500/month
- small vessels (short distance)       US$300/month
- cabotage (6,7 crews)                 US$150/month
- smallest boats                           free
(c)  Tariff M3 (unloading fresh fish)
- Fresh catch for humin use            US$ 2.5/ton
(about 1Z fish value FOB)
- Anchoita for animal use              US$ 0.4/ton
(about 12 of fish value sold)
(d)  Tariff M4 (loading, unloading on the port facility)
- Frozen fish                          US$ 4/ton
- Powdered fish                        US$1.2/ton
- Fish oil                             US$1.5/ton
A 102 tariff increase was assumed in 1997 and in 2000.
7.        Concerning Piriapolis the port capacity is considered as:
- Berthing                           112 boats
- Mooring                             21 boats
- Storage and repair yard            120 boats
- Fishing vessels                       5 boats
- Average length of boat              11.2 meters
The demand is estimated to be 218 to 432 boats during 1991 to 2000 and 437
boats in the year 2000. The tariff for Pitiapolis is taken as 902 of rates
used in Punta del Este.   Real  term  tariff increases were assumed as 102 in
1995 and in 1999  and  202  in  2003.    Also,  the revenues from recovery of
appreciation of land and net leasing were increased by 302 in 1999. It is
assumed that Piriapolis should  start  earning  revenues  from 1993.  Average
berthing of boats was assumed to be 30 days. Visitors to Piriapolis were
assumed as:
- Numbers                            400
- Days per year                        60
- Average cost US$ per day         12.48
The revenues for water and electricity supply were calculated using same
rates as of supply companies and are similar to rates in Punta del Este.

(v) Operating Costs
8.        La Paloma - presently there are 10  employees at the port and 12 in
the dredging operation. Dredging staff would be eliminated since no dredging
would be required  after  the  breakwater  is  constructed.    The port would
increase its staff strongth from 10 employees to 12.
9.        The wage increase in real term  was  assumed  to be 32 in the years
1991, 1994 and 1997. Material and supplies for 1988 were estimated to be the
same as in 1987, adjusted by  inflation.   From 1990 onwards, the consultants
estimated N$ 5.0 million pesos p.a.
10.       Punta del Este - Presently,  34  regular and 23 temporary staff are
engaged.  In the future, 40 permanent  and 28 temporary (or 7 equivalent full
time) staff are assumed. Material and supplies cost are estimated based on
past experience.
11.       Piriapolis - The average operating  costs  of this port are assumed
same as Pta. del Este (about N$112,500) per year including embarkation in
water (berthing and mooring). Number of employees taken as 5.
(vi) Depreciation
12.       For La Paloma the historical value of assets of US$17.5 million was
used and asset life assumed as 50 years (17.5 million - 50 years = US$350,000
or N$111,000,000). The depreciation was adjusted upward to N$153.5 million
to include the new investments.
13.       For Punta del  Este,  the  historical  value  of  assets of US$14.1
million was used and asset life assumed at 100 years. The new depreciation
was calculated at N$57 million starting in 1991.
14.       For Piriapolis, the depreciation on the breakwater and the dike are
taken on 33Z of the capital because the regular annual repairs are charged to
expense.   Only  major  repairs  (e.g.,  due  to  stoLms)  are  assumed to be
capitalized and therefore depreciated.   The  existing assets were assumed to
have 50 years of life and 502 of depreciation was allocated to the existing
breakwater. From proposed investment, 671 is for the breakwater and the
dike. Therefore 33Z of the breakwater portion (i.e. 2/3 of total it.vestment)
was used for depreciation with 75 years life expectancy.
