URUGUAY
TRANSPORT PROJECT I
RLview of the Transport Sector Expenditure Program
A. Overview
(a) The Revised 1987-89 Transport Program
1.       The revised  1967-89  transport  sector  program  of  about US$317
million (Table 1 of this Annex), agreed with OPP in March 1988, would
exceed the original limit set by OPP in 1987 by about 301. The difference
is due mostly to increased allocations to DNV which has demonstrated a
slightly higher implementation capacity than was originally estimated. The
overall size of the transport program, about 1.5Z of GDP and 30X of total
public sector investments, is appropriate considering the past neglect of
infrastructure and current needs.    The  relative amounts allocated to the
various modes also generally correctly reflect their economic role and
needs: about 74' of the the  total  is  for highways, 15? for ports, 7? for
railways and 3X for the National Directorate of Transport to over sea
transport operations and regulations.   Tihe  programs are based in the case
of roads, on carefully analyzed economic feasibility studies and priority
ranking, and, in the case of ANP and AFE, on consultant master plan
studies.
The program for small craft and fishing ports, however, includes several
investments such as those proposed for Puerto Viejo de Colonia, the
Atlantida Port and for the purchase of a dredger for which economic
justification has not yet  been  established.   The National Directorate of
Hydrography (DNH) intends to update or carry out economic feasibility
studies for these investments and implement them only if they demonstrate
adequate economic returns (not less than 12?).
2.       Implementation of the  1987-89  transport  program  has been slow,
reaching only about 50? of the  target  for 1987 due mostly to insufficient
implementation capacity in DNH, ANP and PLUNA, but also to uncertainties
about the future role and direction of ANP and AFE. Only DNV slightly
exceeded its  program.    Except  for  DNV,  not  much  improvement  in the
implementation rate is expected until 1989 when the future of ANP and AFE
would have been clearly decided.
(b)  MTOP's Transport Sector Proposals for 1989-92
3.       The  transport  agencies   are   currently  preparing  expenditure
proposals for 1988-1992 (Tables 2 through 4 of this Annex) which call for
some 700 million over the period or an average of USD140 million p.a.,
which would be some 32? above OPP's currently approved yearly averages for
1987-89. The relative distribution among agencies/subsectors remains the
same as in the  1987-89  which  is  acceptable.    The size of the proposed
program may, however, exceed the implementation capacity of all the
agencies as well as Government's financial capacity and would need to be
carefully studied by the office of Planning and Budgeting (OPP).



94 -                          ANNEX 5
Page 2 of 3
4.       The  following  are  the   mission's   specific  comments  on  the
individual modal programs:
(i) DNV's Road Program (Table  2):    is  in  line with needs, is
balanced with respect to construction vs. maintenance (about
532 for maintenance and 47? for construction) and comprises
projects demonstrated to be economically justifiable. The
program's size, about an average of US$102 million a year
may, however, be beyond DNV's implementation capacity. This
point should be examined during OPP's review.
(ii) ANP Montevideo Port Program (Table 3): is based on the
recommendations from consultants INTECSA's Montevideo Master
Plan Studies, is limited to investmencs to be financed by the
proposed Transport Project I, include only economically
justifiable projects with very high economic returns an..; is
within the OPP-approved annual  ceilings  for ANP.  The major
investments contemplated include:  construction  of a closing
dike (US$3.0 million, ERR  =  76Z), remodeling of the general
cargo terminal (US$2.9 million), remodeling of the perishable
goods  terminal  (US$1.2   million),   supply  of  additional
electrical power  and  container  handling  equipment (US$4.3
million) and a technical assistance and staff training
program (US$2.6 million).    Should  the Government decide to
cut the transport      sector expenditures, the ANP component
should be left intact because of (a) its high returns and (b)
the crucial need for the investments in order to transform
ANP into a profitable commercially-viable entity.
(iii) DNH's Program (Table 4): DNH's proposed program of US$71.4
million includes eight major items plus an unallocated amount
of US$32 million of which only the expansion of Punta del
Este,  Piriapolis  and   the   La   Paloma  ports  have  been
demonstrated to be economically justifiable (see para. 1 of
this annex). DNH intends to revise existing studies for some
of the items and initiate new ones for the rest.
(iv) AFE's Program (Table 6): AFE's proposal of US$46 million is
likely to be reduced to about US$37 million due to the
slippages in the 1987 and 1988 programs. The US$37 million
would be in line with the annual figures approved by OPP for
the 1987-89 period.
(v) PLUNA's Program: PLUNA h:-s not yet prepared a program for
1988-92.   Given  its  slow  rate  of  implementation  of the
1987-89 program, which started only in 1988, it is unlikely
that it would invest more than US$7 million during 1988-92.
B. Institutional Arrangements for Public Expenditure Management
5.       OPP is responsible  for  the  overall  management of public sector
investments including coordinating in:estments, monitoring implementation
progress and reviewing updated investment programs during implementation.
The sectoral ministries are responsible for developing sectoral policies to



- 95 -                          ANNEX 5
Page 3 of 3
guide the .lvestment priorities of their agencies. Specifically for the
transport sector, MTOP has established a planning nucleus within the
ministry, with the tec!nical support of it.3 Assesoria de Evaluacion
Tecnico- Economica (AETE), to formulate the investment guidelines for the
agencies and to review and integrate their proposals. A representative of
OPP participates in the  work  of  the  nucleus.   Each transport agency or
directorate is expected to have an investment planning unit to develop its
proposal. In practice only DNV has this unit. The rest have used some ad
hoc consultant's assistance to develop their investment programs, which is
a good first step, but they need to develop the in house capacity for
continuous program updating. The proposed project would provide technical
assistance to strengthen planning units ano train staff in DNV, ANr, DNH as
well as at the  level  of  HTOP.    In  tne  meantime  AETE, which has some
experienced staff, would be given a broader role in assisting ANP, AFE and
DNH and in integrating  the  agencies'  proposals.   AETE would require the
addition of two or three experienced economists/planners to fulfill such a
role adequately.
C. Funding of the 1989-92 Transport Program
6.       Financing for the proposed 1988-92 public sector transport program
is expected to be from the following sources: FIHTOP (43Z); international
and bilateral agencies such as t:e Bank, IDB, Japan Exim Bank and PNUD
(282); the Treasury (28Z); and FONPLATA (insignificant). The commitments
from the IDB, and PNUD have  a'ready  been secured while that from the Bank
and the Japan Exim bank would be funded through the proposed project. Less
certain are the resources from FIMTOP and the treasury which would need to
be confirmed based upon the existing expenditure ceilings on public sector
spending agreed with the IHF.

UtUGUAY
TRANSPORT PROJECT I
The Institutional Development Program
I. ROADS
A.   Technical Assistance to MTOP (Roads; see IIA for Ports)
Background:   The  Third  Highway  Project  (Ln 2238-UR) provided
funds to strengthen MTOP's  capabilities of transport planning, programming
and investment.    This  technical  assistance  is  aimed  at expanding and
improving these skills.
(i) Transportation Planning
Objectives:  The main  objective  of this technical assistance is
to provide MTOP with Consultant services to prepare and put into
operation a transportation planning model (TPM) and training
programa for management personnel.
Scope: To this end the services will:
(a)  introduce  modern   transportation   policy,   planning  and
budgeting procedures for the administration and planning of
the national intermodal transportation networks;
(b) assist in the preparation of a road user charges study;
(c) assist in the selection, procurement and operation of
computers and software suitable for transportation network
analysis and planning;
(d) prepare a manual for the operation of the TPM;
(e) prepare on-the-job training in intermodal trip assignment
models, transportation, inventories, traffic volume models
and economic analysis models;
(f) recommend organizational changes required for operation and
implementation of the TPM; and
(g)  assist MTOP in  the  preparation  of  a suitable program for
management training including seminars, fellowships and
special conferences.
Cost and Timing:
Consultants:  75 man-months US$4,000 plus taxes         US$363,000
Computer equipment and software                         US$ 10,000
Conferences and technical visits abroad 45xUS$2,000   US$ 90,000
Total                                                   US$463,000
The consultants would start work by July 1, 1989 and complete it
by December 31, 1991.



- 103 -                         ANNEX 6
Page 2 of 15
B.   Technical Assistance to DNV
Background:   The  Third  Highway  Project  (Ln 2238-UR) provided
funds for DNV to strengthen their capabilities in maintenance, and
equipment  management,  traffic  sa ety, highway  planning  and  soils and
materials investigation. This technical assistance is aimed at e"cpanding
and improving these skills.
(i) Highway Planning
Objectives: To provide DNV with consultant services to prepare
and put into operation a highway planning model (HPM)
Scope: To this end, the service will:
(a)  introduce  modern  highway   planning   procedures  for  the
administration and planning of the national highway network
and for the preparation of a rolling Five-Year Investment
and Maintenance Program;
(b) assist in the selection, procurement and operation of
computers and software suitable for highway network analysis
and planniag;
(c) prepare a manual for the operation of the HPM;
id)  prepare  on-the-job  training  in  trip  assignment  models,
highway inventories, traffic volume models and economic
analysis models;
(e) redefine primsry, secondary and tertiary network and levels
of investments for each network; and
(f) recommend organizational changes required for operation of
the HPM.
Cost and Timing:
Consultants: 24 man-months x US$4,000 plus taxes      US$116,000
Computer equipmcnt and software                       US$ 15,000
Training abroad: 15 man months x US$2,000              US$30,000
Total                                                 US$161,000
The consultants would start work by July 1, 1989 ar,d complete it
by December 31, 1990.
(ii) Soils and materials investigation
Objectives: To provide DNV with consultant services to expand
and improve the standardized test procedures, specifications,
analysis and quality controls for design, construction and
maintenance projects.



- 104 -                         ANNEX 6
Page 3 of 15
Scope: To this end, the service will:
(a)  introduce  modern  procedures  for  the  administration  and
planning of  the  central  and  field  lab  units to provide
adequate quality control for the project;
(b) assist in the selection, procurement and operation of lab
equipment and introduction of new testing procedures;
(c) prepare a manual for standardization oi test procedures,
materials specifications and quality control;
(d) prepare on-the-job training in test procedures and analysis;
and
(e) provide legal assistance to DNV to establish procedures for
the rejection of, or penalties for, works or materials which
test below the project specifications;
Cost and Timing:
Consultants: 24 man-months x US$4,000 plus taxes        US$116,000
Laboratory equipment                                    US$245,000
Survey equipment                                        US$120,000
Computer and Design equipment and software              US$118,000
Training abroad 32 man months x US$2,000                US$ 64,000
Total                                                   US$663,000
The consultar.ts would start work by July 1, 1989 and complete it
by June 30, 1990.
(iii)  Traffic Management
Objectives: To provide DNV with consultant services to assist
DNV in the improvement of its traffic management.
Scope: To thi end, the service will:
(a)  introduce  modern  traffic  management  procedures  for  the
administration and planning of a comprehensive traffic
operation program to improve the operation and safety of the
national highway system;

C.   Technical Assistance to DNV and Participating Departments.
Background:   The  Third  Highway  Project  (Ln 2238-UR) provided

supervision agency, and participating departments, which were the executors
of the National Feeder Rords  Program.   This technical assistance is aimed
at increasing the departments responsibility in maintenance of roads within
their departments with the cooperation of DNV and in reducing the costs of
financing duplicate functions for road maintenance in each department.

II. PORTS
A. Technical Assistance to Redefine the Government's Role at Small
Craft Ports and to Reorganize Dredging Activities
Background:   Small craft  ports and harbors are currently built,
administered and operated by MTOP, through DNH. The current situation has
proved to be satisfactory at Punta del Este, but requires further
consideration at other existing and future locations, as this may impose
undesirable constraints to private investments. The Action Plan includes a
revision of the existing policy through an analysis of the Government's
present monopolistic situation vis-a-vis the Government monitoring and
promoting tourism development with private sector participation, either as
developers  and  operators   or   as  concessionaries  of  Government-owned
facilities. The Port Action Plan also provides for the separation of
dredging  activities  from  ANP's  responsibilities.    Consultants  to  be
retained for the improvement of ANP's organization and functions would make
specific recommendations with regard to this activity. However, additional
technical assistance would also be required for the actual organization of
a separate  dredging  entity.    In  addition  to  the  above the technical
assistance would include the development and implementation of commercially
based general and cost accounting systems, a cost-based tariff structure
and a billing system for the small craft ports anl harbors that would
remain under DNH.
(i) Small Craft Ports
Oblectives: The main objective of this technical assistance component
is to analyze alternative arrangements to promote, develop and operate



- 108 -                         ANt1EX 6
Page 7 of 15
small craft ports and harbors to ensure their financial self-
sufficiency,  attract  private   investments  and  ensure  operational
efficiency.
Scope.   The  consultants  would  critically  analyse  the investments
undertaken by the Government to develop, maintain and operate existing
infrastructure; identify investments made that are not economically
and financially justified; identify potential participation of private
sector either in infrastructure development or in provision of public
services;  recommend  alternative   mechanisms   to   allow  for  such
participation; recommend actions required to transfer some of the
existing facilitips to the private sector on a concessionary bas:is and
to promote new wider participation of the private sector; and develop
and implement general and cost accounting systems for the main small
craft ports.
Cost and Timing:
Consultants: 4 man-months (foreign) x US$9,000 plus taxes  US$ 43,560
10 man-months (local) x US$3,000 plus taxes    US$ 36,300
Reimbursable expenses plus taxes                             US$ 20,140
US$100,000
The consultants would start work by April 1, 1989 and complete it
by December 31, 1989.
(ii) Organization of Dredging Activities
Objectives: The main objective of this technical assistance component
would be to separate dredging activities from ANP and establish a
commercially oriented enterprise to carry out all maintenance dredging
at national level and to recover the costs involved through users
charges.
Scope:  Phase 1  - Diagnostic.    The  consultants would carry out an
assessment of Uruguay maintenance dredging requirements and of the
existing dredging capabilities both in terms of dredging capacity,
taking into consideration the dredging plan for Montevideo prepared
under the Master Plan Study, and of remaining economic life of the
existing equipment plant.    The  consultants  would  also analyze the
technical skills of personnel assigned to this activity, outline a
dredging plan for the next 5 years, based on ongoing port and river
investment plans, evaluate alternative schemes to organize dredging
activities, and define required Government actions to implement each
of such alternatives. This phase is estimated to last about 8 weeks
after which the consultants would prepare an Inception Report.
Phase  2  -  Design  and  Implementation.       Upon  selection  by the
Government of the best alternative, the consultants would develop and
implement   the   proposed   structural   organization   of   the  new
commercially-oriented and financially self-sufficient dredging entity.
They would develop a detailed dredging program including required
investments, if any, to ensure that the dredging entity is capable of
implementing such dredging program with the existing dredging fleet or
identify the need to complement it with additional equipment or



- 109 -                         ANNEX 6
Page 8 of 15
dredging contractors.   The  consultants  would  carry  out on the job
training of managerial staff assigned to dredging activities within
the entity. This phase is estimated to last about 6 months after
which the consultants would submit a Draft Final Report, followed by a
Final Report incorporating the Government comments and observations.
Cost and Timing:
Consultants:  6 man-months (foreign) x US$9,000 plus taxes   US$65,340
8 man-months (local) x US$3,000 plus taxes       US$29,040
Reimbursable Expenses plus taxes                               US$26,620
Total                                                         US$121,000
The consultants would start on May 1, 1989 and complete the work
by December 31, 1989.
B. Technical Assistance to ANP
Background: ANP's organization is obsolete, functions are poorly
defined and include a number of activities not directly related to port
administration and operation. Little attention is paid to commercial and
operational  functions  necessary   to   achieve   financial  autonomy  and
operational efficiency. Port productivity at Montevideo, ranks among the
lowest in the region, despite the fact that the port is in general,
overdimensioned and overequipped for current and forecast traffic up to
1995. The reasons for the low productivity levels are complex, ranging
from  inappropriate  management  practices   coupled   with  lack  of  port
operations planning, lack of stevedoring and port labor coordination and
payment systems to workers that promote inefficiency, and poor attention to
commercial practices to educate and incentivate port users.
(i) Improvement to ANP's Organization and Functions
Objectives: The main objective of the technical assistance component
is to assist ANP in the definition, development and implementation of
a structural organization designed to transform it into a (state-
owned) commercial enterprise. To this effect, the consultants to be
retained would take, as the basis for their work, the port policies
and directives contained in the Action Plan, the traffic forecasts,
characteristics of the commercial port system and the recommendations
of the studies carried out under loan 1798-UR, as well as those
resulting from the organizational study being financed through loan
2238-UR. Their responsibilities would include recommendations for the
design  and  implementation  of  a   new  organization  for  ANP,  the
redefinition of functions and responsibilities  at all levels, and the
subsequent assignment of  the  necessary  human resources required for
activities directly related with  port  planning, administration, and
with commercial, financial and operational functions. The consultants
would make specific recommendations with regard to the transfer of all
other activities to third parties.
Scope: The work would be implementation oriented and include the
(a)  definition  of  an   organization   scheme   appropriate  to  the
activities that would remain under ANP's responsibility;



110 -                        ANNEX 6
Page 9 of 15
(b) definition of functions and responsibilities at all managerial
levels  and  the   interaction   among   the  various  ports  and
departments within ANP, as well as the coordination and links
with MTOP and other Government agencies; and
(c) development and implementation of the organizational scheme,
including preparation of corresponding manuals and procedures,
and on-the-job training at managerial levels.
Cost and Timing:
Consultants: 25 man-months (foreign) x US$9,000 plus taxes US$260,000
40 man-months (local) x US$3,000 plus taxes   US$145,000
Reimbursable expenses plus taxes               US$ 79,000
Total                                          US$484,000
The consultants would start work by March 1, 1989 and complete it
by June 30, 1990.
(ii) Marketing
Objectives:    ANP's  operation   needs   to   be  upgraded  based  on
commercially oriented criteria. An important element to enable ANP to
carry out business efficiently and profitably is the utilization of
proper marketing and commercial research in local and foreign markets
to define characteristics, clients' needs, competition factors, etc.
This would result in proper definition of marketing objectives and
strategies.
Scope: This technical assistance component would be addressed to
defining the size, structure and trends of current and potential
markets through marketing research, with the purpose of identifying
the level of demand and characteristics of competitive ports. Based
on the above the consultants would define objectives and marketing
strategies and evaluate the cost of improvement of services offered,
including alternative commercial incentives to attract additional
traffic. This technical assistance would include on-the-spot training
of marketing analysts and the development and implementation of
administrative systems of ANP's Commercial Department.
Cost and Timing:
Consultants:  4 man-months (foreign) x US$9,000 plus taxes   US$41,850
3 man-months (local) x US$3,000 plus taxes      US$10,890
Reimbursable Expenses plus taxes                               US$ 7,260
Total                                                         US$60,000
The consultants would start work by January 1, 1990 and r;omplete
it by June 30, 1990.
(iii)  Improvement to ANP's Port Operations
Objectivest The objectives of this technical assistance component
are:     to   increase   operational   productivity;  rationalize  the



- 111 -                         ANNEX 6
Page 10 of 15
utilization of existing infrastructure, equipment and human resources;
coordinate port services with users' needs and customs regulations;
simplify port documentations and develop appropriate cargo control
system.
Scope: To Achieve the foregoing objectives, the consultants' services
would be divided in the following phases:
Phase 1 - Diagnostic. analysis of present and forecast traffic
composition, cargo-handling systems and controls and of current
operational procedures; identification of major problems affecting
port operations with a view to define alternative Government and/or
ANP actions that may be required to solve them; identify improvements
that can be implemented immediately and prepare a plan of action for
that purpose; assess capabilities and skills of human resources at all
levels assigned to port operations including ANSE personnel. At the
end of this Phase which is expected to last six weeks, the consultants
would prepare an Inception Report including actions required and
details of works to be executed during the Phase 2 of the technical
assistance.
Phase 2 - Design.    Implementation  of  the immediate plan of action;
preparation of a plan for the rationalization of port operations,
personnel and equipment in specialized port areas; preparation of port
operations planning and regulations, procedures and manuals; analysis
of the viability of concessioning selected port services; development
and implementation of simplified port documentation; development of
port productivity monitoring systems and of a productivity based
compensation system for workers.   This  phase is estimated to last 12
weeks.  Upon its  completion,  the  consultants  shall submit a report
covering, among other things,  the  results  of  the immediate plan of
action and the operations rationalization plan.
Phase 3  -  Implementation.    Development  and  implementation of the
rationalization  plan,   including   manpower,   equipment,   and  the
monitoring systems and controls.   This  Phase is estimated to last 20
weeks, at the end of which the consultants will present a Draft Final
Report.
Phase 4 - Post Evaluation.   Six  months after completing Phase 3, the
consultants would asspss the results of this technical assistance
component and review and revise as necessary the system, controls and
practices used. This phase 4 is estimated to last about one month,
after  which  the   consultants   would   present   the  Final  Report
incorporating the Bank and ANP comments, an evaluation of results
obtained and the additional recommendations resulting from this final
phase.
Cost and Timing:
Consultants: 20 man-months (foreign) x US$9,000 plus taxes US$218,000
30 man-months (local) x US$3,000 plus taxes   US$109,000
Reimbursable expenses plus taxes                               US$ 36,000
US$363,000
The consultants would start work by July 1, 1989 and complete
Phase 3 by April 30, 1990, and Phase 4 by October 31, 1990.

(iv) Reorganization of ANP's Maintenance Services
Obiectives: This technical assistance component would be addressed to
reorganize port maintenance services in line with ANP's operational
and administrative services requirements, arising from the revised
functions and responsibilities, and to increase equipment availability
to  acceptable   international   levels   (i.e.,   80?   in  operating
conditions).
Scope:  to achieve the foregoing objectives, tle consultants' services
would be divided in the following phases:
Phase 1 - Diagnostic. Analysis of present plant of equipment and its
adequacy to cope with operational demands; development of a detailed
inventory of physical  conditions;    analysis  of  physical and human
resources  allocated   to   equipment   maintenance   and  other  port
maintenance; analysis of current organization of maintenance services;
identification  of  problems   and  bottlenecks  affecting  day-to-day
maintenance; and preparation of an immediate plan of action to remedy
such problems and bottlenecks. This phase is expected to last about 5
weeks after which the consultants will prepare an Inception Report,
including actions required and details of works to be executed during
the second phase.
Phase 2 - Design and Implementation of the Immediate Plan of Action:
Preparation of a plan for the rationalization of port maintenance,
including reorganization, accounting and costing systems, equipmeiit
utilization and inventory controls, budgetary requirements, training
needs and maintenance data systems.    The  plan will also include all
necessary manuals, regulations and monitoring systems. Simultaneously
with the preparation of this plan, the consultants will assist their
counterparts in the implementation of the plan of action. Phase 2 is
expected to last about 16 weeks upon which completion the consultants
shall submit a report with their recommendations and the results of
the plan of action.
Phase 3  -  Implementation:    Development  and  implementation of the
rationalization plan.  This phase is estimated to last about 20 weeks,
at the end of which the consultants shall submit a Draft Final Report
containing a detailed  analysis  of  results obtained and highlighting
problems still to be solved to ensure the achievement of the
objectives.
Phase 4 - Post evaluation:    Six  months after completion of Phase 3,
the consu.cants would carry out  a  critical assessment of results and
review, revise and implement all  necessary changes and adjustments to
ensure satisfactory results. This phase is expected to last about 4
weeks after which the consultants would present the Final Report
incorporating the Bank and ANP comments, the evaluation of results,
and any additional  recommendation  of  actions/decisions  that may be
required.
Consultants: 20 man-months (foreign) x US$9,000 plus taxes US$218,000
30 man-months (local) x US$3,000 plus taxes   US$109,000

The consultants would start work by March 1, 1989 and complete it
by December 31, 1989.
(v) Improvement to ANP's Port Financial and Management Information
Systems
Objectives: The first objective of this technical assistance is to
strengthen  internal  auditing   and   control,  long  term  strategic
financial planning, tariff restructuring based on realistic operating
costs, and management information. The technical assistance will also
include checking of the costing system recently established by
expatriate consultants to ensure that it is functioning well, as the
results of this system will become a reliable base for tariff setting.
Financial accounting and cost accaunting records would be fully
integrated to ensure accounting, costing and budgetary controls are
effective  and  reliable.    The  second  objective  would  consist of
improving the management information system so that it becomes a
management tool for somnd policy and decision making.
Scope: The consultants who will be engaged for these jobs will ensure
that methodologies and techniques introduced by them are modern,
compatible and practical for  ANP.    The consultants will ensure that
suitable counterparts are selected to be trained by them in different
functions. All procedural manuals will be well documented and left
with trained counterparts for their use in the future. Consultants
will keep in mind the concept of responsibility accounting for each
function so    that  financial  viability  of  ANP  remains always the
primary  objective.    The  consultants  will  design  the  management
information systems for all levels of management in formats suitable
for their needs and in various timing and details required by them.
The consultants will focus on the following main tasks:
(a) Costing System - Under the previous loan (1798-UR) a costing
system was designed  and  established  together with improvements
and  modified  categories   in   the   accounting  system.    The
counterpart team was trained and  the  manuals were written.  The
consultants  will  ensure  that  such  a  costing  system  is  in
operation   and   particularly    that   basic   procedures   are
computerized. The consultants will take all necessary actions to
eliminate problems and deficiencies still being experienced.
(b) Marginal Cost Concept - Once the consultants are satisfied with
the new costing system, they will strengthen it by introducing
the long run marginal  cost  concept.    The  unit costs for each
activity or cost center would then reflect marginal costs and
total costs to enable the port management to set adequate tariffs
considering costs and competition with neighboring ports. The
unit costs will be split into labor, material and other costs.
The consultants will also introduce a standard costing system to
help management control costs, rationalize various operations in
the port area and to strengthen the budgeting control system.
This  would  enable   management   to  evaluate  each  activity's
performance and introduce further improvements in cost control.



- 114 -                        ANNEX 6
Page 13 of 15
(c) Restructuring of Tariffs - The consultants will carefully examine
the existing tariff policy. In particular, they will ensure that
all disparities in the present policy are removed (such as higher
import than export  tariffs,  innappropriate  transit and fishing
vessel tariffs).  Tariffs  should  reflect the costs of efficient
operations.    They  should  make  specific  recommendations when
necessary.
(d) Internal Audit/Control - ANP's Internal Audit Unit is weak and
understaffed. Consultants will ensure that the Internal Audit
function becomes very effective and functions as a good liaison
between ANP's management and the external auditors. Consultants
will make recommendations and implement them as necessary. It
will be ensured that internal audit procedures are properly
written and the staff is adequately trained.
(e) Long-Term Strategic Investment and Financial Planning - Presently
no  long  term  strategic   financial  planning  exists  in  ANP.
Consultants will introduce a suitable investment and financial
planning system which should become a useful tool for the
management  to  make  sound   judgements  and  future  investment
decisions.   The  analytical  tools  and  methodology  to be used
should  be  based   on   the   latest  approach  and  techniques.
Consultants will train  the  counterparts  in  this area and will
write procedural manuals for their future use.
(f)  Mechanization of Information System  - Consultants will recommend
and install a suitable mechanized reporting system. They will
recommend ANP what type of computers and software to use. They
will establish a system of information flow and production of
prompt  and  reliable   statistical   operational  and  financial
reports.
(g) Management Information System - The consultants would strengthen
the existing information system.   They will establish sources of
each type of information to avoid duplications and conflicts,
design suitable formats of each type of report and computerize
their processing. The periodic reports will be for two levels of
management. One type of reports will be for top management which
would highlight main events in manpower, operations, traffic,
marketing, engineering and maintenance, planning, and finance and
budgeting. The purpose of the report would be to inform about
the  activities  as   well   as   to   alert  the  management  of
irregularities,  thus  enabl'.ng  them   for  their  major  policy
decisions and/or remedial  actions.    The  other type of reports
will be for  the  mid-managerial  and  supervisory levels.  These
reports will cover  more  details  of  the  activities under each
responsibility center. The accountability of each major job will
be clearly  identified.    The  periodic  reports  that should be
prepared would cover areas such as: tonnage handled by ships,
productivity per gang hour, ship waiting time, gangs employed,
revenues and costs, cash flow, traffic by categories, number of
machines in workshop, container terminal activities, and berth
occupancy.



- 115 -                        ANNEX 6
Page 14 of 15
Consultants will recommend and it.stall a suitable mechanized
reporting  syscem  that  will   include  suitable  computers  and
software. Tnis facility should enable ANP to pruduce useful
information promptly and accurately.
Cost and TiminR:
Consultants: 20 man-months (foreign) x US$9,000 plus taxes US$218,000
30 man-months (local) x US$3,000 plus taxes   US$109,000
Computer equipment and software                               US$300,000

C. ANP's Training Program
Background: The upgrading of ANP's training organization started
in 1987 through the s,ssistanice provided by UNDP-ILO, which established a
Training Center and iiitiated a  twoo-year  program aimed at improving a the
performance of approximately 1,400 employees. The proposed reorganization
of ANP contemplated ln the Action Plan would include the establishment of a
Training Steering Corimittee and the strengthening of the existing Training
Center, which should be directly responsible to the Deputy General Manager.
Training  activities  at  local  level  would  be  executed  using existing
training facilities and equipping them  properly through the proposed loan.
Agreement with UNDP and its agencies to review and expand the proposed
four-year training program, and to coordinate, administer and implement
such program, would be necessary in view of ANP's limited experience and
qualified personnel to  undertake  those  tasks.   Training of supervisors,
equipment operatore and port workers would be carried out in Montevideo
through courses and seminars. Training of top and mid-level managers would
be carried out through specialized courses and seminars in Montevideo and
abroad. Preparation of professionals in port administration and planning
would be achieved thxrough scholarship abroad.
Objectives. The project's port training component aims at:
(a) supporting ANP institutional reform and changes through the
establishment  of   a   Training   Steering   Committee  and  the
strengthening  of  the   existing   Training  Center  within  the
organizational structure of ANP; and
(b) strengthening the performance capabilities of ANP's personnel
from managerial levels, to professionals, administrative staff,
supervisors and port workers; and
(c) contributing to the achievement of port productivity targets.
Description: A four-year preliminary training program for the period
1989-1992 has been outlined (Tables 2 and 3 of this annex) in the
cortext of the Government and ANP's institutional reform and changes
of the port subsector contained in the corresponding Action Plan.
Under agreement with one or more technical assistance .nstitutions,



- 116 -                         ANNEX 6
Page 15 of 15
the Training Center would be strengthened to implement a truining
program aimed to improve the performance capabilities of about 2,700
employees, or 56l of ANP's present number of staff. The training
program would include courses and seminars in Uruguay and abroad,
attendance to specialized international conferences, scholarships,
study visits abroad, on-the-job training and training materials and
equipment.   In  addition,  the  training  program  would  include the
technical support required to develop and implement the program and
the improvement of ANP's Training Center.
Execution and  administration  of  the  Training  Program.   ANP would
remodel the necessary areas to improve training facilities and would
enter a contract with  one  or  more technical assistance institutions
for the administration and implementation of the training program.
Provisions  would  be  made  to  hire  foreign  specialists  and local
consultants to  train  ANP  instructors,  select  courses and seminars
abroad, organize local courses and seminars, select candidates for
scholarships, arrange  working  visits  to  selected  ports abroad and
provide all  required  training  material  and  equipment.  ANP would:
(i) establish a Training Committee to deal with training policies,
strategies and priorities; and .ii) strengthen Training Center in
terms s,atisfactory to the  Bank.    This  unit  would be the technical
assistance's counterpart and wauld be expanded as necessary during the
imple2entation of  the  program.    The  basic  cost  of  the training
component is estimated at US$1.1 million equivalent and details are
given in Table 3 of this annex.

ANNEX 7

URUGUAY
TRANSPORT PROJECT I
Summary of Economic Justificat_v.
A. General
1.       Reports  on  the  economic  feasibility  studies,  carried  out by
consultants, for the major project components, are included in the Project
File  (Annex 10).    The  following  summary  is  based  upon  the studies'
findings, modified as necessary to reflect updated information as well as
the appraisal mission's judgement.
3.       All costs  and  benefits  used  in  the  analysis  are  based upon
expected April 1988 price. and shadow-prices as necessary. In aggregate,
shadow prices of 70? for labor and 902 for materials have been used.
B. Route 1
2.       Economic studies for  this  component,  carried out by consultants
CSI, INVIAL SENICO, VIALUR, SAE (Uruguay and Argentina), compare the
benefits and costs from: (a) constructing Route 1 on a new agreement vis a
vis continuing the current pavement management strategy for the existing
road; as well as (b) the alternative solution of a new alignment as opposed
to reconstruction within the existing alignment. The benefits considered
in  both  cases  include:    (a) vehicle  operating  costs  (voc)  savings;
(b) accident costs reductions; (c) time savings; (d) avoidance of costly,
frequent  pavement  overlay  and   rehabilitation.         The  costs  comprise
investment and maintenance costs.  Traffic  on the road in the opening year
of 1992 has been estimated to range between a high of 4,515 vehicles per
day (vpd) (of which 352 are heavy vehicles) on the Montevideo side, to
1,290 vpd (of which 38Z are heavy vehicles) on the Colonia end. Traffic
growth has been estimated to be a modest 22 p.a. A detailed analysis for
five homogenous sections of Route 1 showing the traffic, voc savings,
maintenance costs savings, savings from reduction in accident rates and
time savings as well as the cost and benefit streams for the entire road is
presented in the Project File.   The  stream  of costs and benefits for the
road is shown  in  Table 1  of  this  annex.    The  result of the economic
analysis, based upon the above costs and benefits show that constructing
Route 1 as a portland cement concrete road on a new alignment would yield
an economic rate of return (ERR) of about 261 and is, therefore,
economically justifiable. Sensitivity tests on assumptions of (a) a 102
cost overrun, and (b) a 102 reduction in benefits shows that the ERR is
still over 122.
C. Reconstruction/Upgrading of National Paved Roads
4.       Economic studies for Routes 6 and  7, carried out by DNV and AETE,
compare the benefits and costs of reconstructing these roads with the
current strategy of frequent heavy maintenance on the existing roads. The
costs include reconstruction and tmintenance costs while the benefits



- 121 -
ANNEX 7
Page 2 of 6
include: (a) voc savings; (b) savings in maintenance costs; and (c) time
savings. Tables 2 and 3 of this annex show the relevant inputs taken into
consideration in estimating costs and benefits for the analysis as well as
the resulting cost/benefit flows for  the  roads.   The analysis shows that
reconstructing the two roads would yield ERRs of 222 and 182 respectively.
The remaining roads to be included in the project will be selected from the
existing list of roads for which AETE has estimated ERRs ranging between
152 to 902. The economic analysis for any additional road to be included
in the project would be updated by DNV/AETE using the methodology employed
for Routes 6 and 7 above to ensure that its would ERR exceed 12?.
D. Periodic Maintenance Program for Departmental Roads
5.       The periodic maintenance program covers 10,000 km of unpaved roads
dispersed within the country's 18  departments.   Given the large number of
road sections involved (about 500 sections), the relatively low cost per km
(about US$3,200 per km for periodic and US$350 per km/year for routine
maintenance) and the paucity of traffic and surface roughness data on the
roads, it was agreed with DNV that a road by road ERR would not be
estimated. Instead, a threshold type analysis was adopted which consists
of estimating the traffic levels and composition necessary to attain ERRs
of 122 under various conditions of surface roughness, roadway geometrical
and estimated periodic and subsequent routine maintenance costs. Each road
is then tested  against  the  threshold  traffic  corresponding to its road
surface and geometric conditions. The list of roads to be included in the
first year periodic maintenance program is based upon: (a) their passing
the threshold test; (b) priority listing provided by the departments; and
(c) traffic levels.   The  threshold  ERRs  were  obtained by comparing the
periodic and routine maintenance costs with the benefits from reduction in
vehicle operating costs.   The  voc  savings  were calculated using the HDM
vehicle operating cost model, taking into consideration road surface
roughness and geometric  characteristics.    A  detailed description of the
method used by MTOP in the analysis as well as tables showing the
parameters considered are included in MTOP's report 'Evaluacion de Caminos
DepartamentalesT included in the  Project  File.    A  list  of roads to be
included in the periodic maintenance program during the first year is
provided in Table 4  of  this  Annex.    For  roads  to  be included in the
programs for subsequent years the same methodology would be employed and
the cut-off ERR of 122 maintained. Should DNV request the inclusion of new
feeder roads in the program, the roads would be analyzed by the consumer
surplus approach method with which MTOP's AETE is fully conversant.
E. The Montevideo Port Improvements
6.       The  feasibility  studies   for   the   proposed  improvements  at
Montevideo Port were carried out by consultants INTECSA (Spain). The
consultants, based on various port traffic growth assumptions, tested
different alternative investments with a port simulation model to obtain
the optimum economic returns. Details of the simulation model are included
in the consultants report entitled "Plan de Desarrollo a Largo Piazo para
el Puerto de Montevideo" included in the Project File. The salient ports
are summarized below.



- 122 -
ANNEX 7
Page 3 of 6
7.       Closing  Dike:  construction  of  the  closing  dike  would  yield
benefits in the form of:   (a) reduced cost of annual maintenance dredging;
and (b) protection of some 300 hectares for the expansion of ship repair
facilities, etc. Table 5 of this Annex presents the costs and benefit
flows for the assumed 20-year economic life of these investments. The
benefits from reduced maintenance dredging alone would result in an ERR of
762 for these works.
8.       Provision of Additional Electrical  Power Supply: the provision of
additional electrical  power  supply  would:    (a) permit the simultaneous
operation of the container crane and the general cargo cranes, thus,
reducing ship waiting time and cutting down on the period of dead time for
the cargo-handling labor force; (b) prolong the effective operating hours
at the port; (c) reduce pilferage; and (d) reduce existing losses of
refrigerated goods with the frequent power interruptions. In the economic
analysis only the following benefits have been quantified: (a) reduction
in the possible loss of port traffic due to the saturation of the
Montevideo port without the additional electrical supply; (b) savings from
the reduction of ship waiting time; and (c) savings in manpower costs due
to the reduction of dead time.   The  costs  have been taken as the cost of
electrical power generation and distribution and all its associated civil
works and the  operating  costs  of  the  power  supply:    these amount to
US$3,400  and  US$215,000  per   year,respectively.         The  benefits  from
prevention of  diversion  have  been  estimated  by  consultants INTECSA as
US$7.67 per ton additional costs in the alternative ports and US$27.5 per
ton average for  transport  from  and  to  the  other  ports  by road.  The
reduction in ship waiting  time  has  been  costed  at US$5,625 per day for
container ships  of  12,850  TPM,  US$5,663  for  those  of  30,500 TPM and
US$5,880 per day for  container  ships  of  600  TEU capacity.  Lastly, the
benefits from reduced dead time for the container crane crew are costed at
US$83.44 for the crew. Table 6 of this annex presents the calculation of
the different costs and benefits that are used in the economic analysis and
the resulting flow of costs and benefits during the 20-year period of
analysis.  The  investments  for  the  additional  electricity supply would
yield an ERR of 502.
9.       Other Montevideo Port Investments:    The physical improvements in
the container terminal, the remodeling and paving of the general and bulk
cargo areas and of internal roads and the purchase of additional cargo
handling equipment, at an estimated cost of about US$7.45 million, would
yield benefits in  the  form  of:    (a) increased  port productivity, with
reductions in cargo handling costs and ship waiting times; and (b) prolong
the lives of port  equipment.    Owing  to  the difficulty of isolating the
effect of each of these investments on port productivity, consultants
INTECSA used a simulation model for the "with' and "without, project case
to estimate the increase in port productivity and hence savings. Details
of the methodology, the simulation model and its results are included in
the Project File. The results indicated that the investments would yield
an economic return of 46Z.
F. Small Craft Ports
10.      Feasibility studies for the expansion of the Punta Del Este and La
Paloma ports were carried out by consultants CEDEX and INYPSA (Spain). For
the Punta Del Este Port, the proposed construction of additional wharves



- 123 -
ANNEX 7
Page 4 of 6
would result in: (a) increased use of the marina and resulting revenues
from boat charges; (b) spin-off effects from provision of ship services;
and (c) general net spending by  tourists.    Revenues  from the use of the
marina have been based on tariffs of US$4.5 per meter per day for 60 days
and US$1.6 per meter per day  for  30  days.  In all about 110 embarkations
per year of such boats are estimated.   Net benefits from services to boats
are estimated at US$2.3 per day for 20 meter boats and US$1.2 per day for
10 meter boats. For net benefits from general spending by tourists, an
average estimate of US$19.38 per day has been used. Table 7 of this Annex
presents the cost/benefit stream for the 18-year analysis period of the
investment. The investment in the port would yield an ERR of 432. The
construction of the breakwater at the La Paloma fishing port and redredging
of the wharf areas would permit the additional fishing activities being
actively promoted by the Government.   Quantifiable benefits would include:
(a) port charges to fishing boat3; (b) net value of additional fish catch
generated by the improvements;and (c) savings in transport costs to the
Montevideo fishing port which would otherwise be necessary without the La
Paloma investments.   The  assumptions  on  fishery development activities,
projections of fish catches and other parameters entering the economic
analysis  are  detailed  in   the   Consultants'  report  entitled:    "The
Feasibility Studies of the Recreational Ports of Punta del Este and
Piriapolis and the Fishing Port of La Palomal included in the Project File.
The economic return from the investments in the La Paloma port would be
282.
C. Piridpolis Purt Project
11.      Background.  Piriapolis is located  on  the Costa de Oro, some 100
km east of Montevideo and 40 Km west of Punta del Este. Its present
population is estimated at 5,800 inhabitants.   The town was founded in the
early 19008 and became the preeminent summer resort in Uruguay in the
1930s. In the 1950s Piritpolis was overtaken by Punta del Este but the
town still attracts family tourism.   Its infrastructure is gradually being
upgraded and could easily accommodate a substantial increase in tourism.
Even during the 1987 peak tourism season the utilization of the existing
hotel capacity was only about 402.   Piriapolis is well suited for all-year
tourism because of its sheltered location, surrounding natural sites, and a
nearby game park.
12.      The proposed port project would extend an existing pier, construct
another pier, and create a protected port that would be used by pleasure
boats, fishing boats and by a planned ferry from Buenos Aires. The
enlarged port could be expected to reverse the long decline of Piriapolis
and accelerate the already initiated renovation and expansion of the town
itself. Data on foreign tourism arrivals in Uruguay by year and season are
shown in Table 8.
13.      Expected Economic Benefits.  The  construction  of the port can be
expected to produce five benefits.    The  first  benefit (Bi) would be the
direct charges for the  use  of  the  port  that  would be paid by pleasure
boats.  Only 95Z of  these,  assumed  to  be  paid from foreign tourists in
foreign exchange, have been included.   Discounted at 122 Bl represents 102
of total benefits. The second benefit (B2) comprises the value added @422
of charges for boat repairs and service that the increased port traffic
would generate. Discounted at 12Z B2 represents 2Z of the total benefits.



- 124 -
ANNEX 7
Page 5 of 6
The third benefit (B3) measures the value added @42Z of tfe expenditure of
incremental  pleasure  boat  tourists.    The  daily  expenditure  has been
estimated at US$21 per day per person with 95Z of this expenditure
projected to be from foreign tourists.    Discounted at 122 B3 accounts for
322 of total benefits.   The  fourth  benefit (B4) measures the value added
p422 of the incremental expenditure of non-boat tourists that are attracted
to Piridpolis as a result of the port construction. The daily expenditure
has been estimated at a low US$15 per tourist, in recognition of the fact
that Piriapolis non-boat tourists will be lower spenders than Punta del
Este tourists. Discounted at 122 B4 represents 372 of total benefits. The
fifth benefit (B5) comprises the value added of the expenditure from short-
term foreign tourists that will visit Piri4polis as a result of a planned
car-ferry that will operate between Buenos Aires and Piri§polis. Only half
of the expenditure of the projected visitors has been included. Discounted
at 122 B5 amounts to 182 of total benefits.
14.      Estimated Economic Costs.  The  economic costs have been estimated
as 672 of the financial incremental investment costs (Cl) plus financial
operations and  maintenance  costs  (C2).    The  correction  factor of 672
summarizes the effect of subtracting 212 on account of the value added tax.
other taxes and import duties, and shadow-pricing labor the nominal wage
rates at 48Z. Both the existing infrastructure and the hotels will be able
to accommodate the incremental tourism projected without any capacity
increase.
15.      Benefit/Cost Comparison.  The comparison between economic benefits
and costs is shown in Table 9. The resulting rate of return is 222 for the
base case, well in excess of the 122 opportunity cost of capital for
Uruguay. Discounted at 122 the net benefits amount to US$4.9 million in
prices of October 1988. The two project value measurements show that the
Piriapolis port project is economically justified.
16.      Sensitivity analysis has been  made  of the economic justification
by varying both benefits and costs.   The result is summarized in Table 10.
The analysis shows that only in the extremely unlikely case of costs
increasing by 202, berthing charges (Bl) decreasing by 102, service charges
(B2) disappearing altogether, pleasure-boat tourist expenditure dropping by
302, other tourism expenditure decreasing by 80Z, and ferry tourism
expenditure falling by 2OZ would the economic rate-of-retufn go below 12z.
17.      Probabilities have also been  assigned  to the different values of
both benefits and costs and the resulting rate-of-retiArn have been
calculated. The combined probability curve drawn in Table 11 shows the
probability that the rate-of-return of the Piritpolis port project will
exceed a certain value.   For  instance,  the probability that the rate-of-
return will exceed 17Z is 0.97, or near certainty.
18.      Foreign Exchange Balance.    The  Piriapolis  port project will be
financed in part by external loans that will later require debt service to
be paid using up  foreign  exchange.    On  the  other  hand, the port will
generate foreign exchange earnings from the tourists attracted to the port.
On balance, the foreign exchange balance will be positive. In 1995, the
year of the highest debt service some US$0.96 million will be required for
debt service. However, in the same year the incremental foreign exchange
expenditure from tourists will reach US$4.69 million, producing a highly
favorable foreign exchange balance.



- 125 -
ANNEX 7
Page 6 of 6
19.      Fiscal Balance.  The net  impact  from the Piriapolis port project
on the Uruguay quasi-fiscal balance will be positive as well. If the
incremental value added tax of 102 of the expenditure is added, a.,suming a
multiplier of 2 from additional expenditures, the incremental tax receipts
to the public treasury would be about US$0.94 milion. This incremental tax
payment will continue during the entire period of operations of the
project.   Discounted  at  12Z  to  the  year  1994,  these incremental tax
payments  amount  to  US$7.6  million.    The  annual  Government financial
contributions to the construction of the port and for the partial payment
of debt service can be similarly discounted at 122 to the year 1994 and
amount to US$6.9 mtllion. The net effect on the public financial position
is thus positive.

URUGUAY
TRANSPORT SECTOR PROJECT
Montevideo Port Traffic Analysis
A. Methodology
1.        The Montevideo  port  handles  both  Uruguay's  import and export
traffic as well as some transit traffic from Argentina, Bolivia, Brazil and
Paraguay. Uruguayan traffic still constitutes the bulk of the Montevideo
port traffic accounting for about 88? of the total (Table 1 of this Annex).
International transit traffic through Montevideo port from the River Plate
Basin countries has, however, been growing rapidly at over 10? p.a. over
the past six years.  The  Montevideo port's main products are: livestock on
the hoof, meat products, fats and oils, textiles, minerals and edible
vegetable products.
2.        The traffic  forecasts  used  for  analyzing  the Montevideo port
improvements under the proposed project were developed by consultants
INTECSA (Spain) as part of their studies for the Montevideo Port Master
Plan. The consultants report on traffic analysis is included in the
Project File.   The  consultants  made  their  forecasts  for Uruguayan and
transit traffic through the Montevideo port based upon: (a) estimated
development of export and imports of the countries involved over the period
1991 to 2013, which were in turn based on a detailed analysis of world
demand projections of the commodities involved and the Governments'
production plans for exports and the economic prospects for the River Plate
Basin countries for imports; and (b) the likely distribution of exports and
imports traffic in the River Plate Basin countries to the ports in the area
based  on  port  capacity,   productivity,  handling  technology,  physical
constraints and the schedules of the international shipping lines. The
consultants   forecast   traffic   for   three   different   scenarios:   a
'pessimistic', a abasea and an 'optimistic" forecast. The 'base' scenario
was used in the economic analysis of the project components as well as in
ANP's financial forecasts.    Sensitivity  tests  were  performed using the
'pessimistic' scenario.
II. Traffic Forecasts
3.        The most pronounced differences  between  the three scenarios are
the  forecasts  of  the  development   of  Argentina's  grain  exports  and
containerized  traffic,  of  Paraguayan   soy   exports  and  the  rate  of
containerization of Uruguayan traffic.   Each  of these factors is analyzed
below.
(i) Argentine Grain Exports
4.        In the 'base'  scenario,  grains  traffic  from Argentina tbirough
Montevideo would increase from its present zero level to about 100,000 tons
in 1991 and 2 million in the year 2010, a growth rate of 100,000 tons



- 138 -                             ANNEX 8
Page 2 of 3
additional per year. In the "pessimistic" case, the corresponding values
would be zero throughout  the  period.    This  attraction of traffic by an
improved Montevideo port would be mostly from the Argentine ports of Buenos
Aires and the Parama ports (except Rosario) since these ports do not have
sufficient draughts to absorb the increasing demand.
(ii) Argentinian Containcr Traffic
5.        Some  of   Argentina's   container   traffic   is  forecasted  by
consultants to be transited through Montevideo port starting in 1991 with
28,000 tons and reaching about 560,000 tons in 2010. This would be traffic
diverted from the Buenos Aires port which has both draught limitations and
very poor conditions  for  container-operation.    Should Argentina move to
increase the drought at the Buenos Aires port or provide sufficient
container-handling capacity in any of its other ports, this expected
transfer to Montevideo port would not occur.
(iii) Uruguayan Grain Traffic
6.        The consultants forecast  a  growth  rate  of  about  42 p.a. for
Uruguayan grains between 1991 &nd 2010 based on world and especially Latin
American demand for grains.    In  the  "pessimistic" case, a corresponding
growth rate of 22 p.a. was assumed.
(iv) Uruguayan Container Traffic
7.        In the "base" case,  Uruguayan  container  traffic is expected to
grow at about 72 p.a. between 1988 and 2008; a reasonable assumption
considering the current low rate of containerization at Montevideo port.
In the pessimistic case the corresponding rates would be slightly lower at
about 62 p.a.
(v) Paraguay's Container Traffic
7.        Paraguay's container traffic through  Montevideo port is expected
under the base scenario to start in 1988 at about 27,000 and increase to
about 180,000 tons in 2010, a growth rate of about 102 p.a. This is based
on the assumption that an improved port can capture all Paraguay's
container traffic in the competition with the Buenos Aires port.
(iv) Total Traffic Projections
8.        The total freight  traffic  through  the  Montevideo  port in the
'base" case is shown  in  Table 4  of  this  Annex.   The following traffic
characteristics are forecasted:
(a) total tonnage would grow from the current 1.36 million tons to
5.41 million tons in 2010; a growth rate of about 6.52 p.a. based
mostly on the expectation of high diversion of Argentina's grain
exports through Montevideo port;



- 139 -                             ANNEX 8
Page 3 of 3
(b) the share of container traffic would increase from the current
17Z to about 30Z in 2010, a low rate of containerization compared
to some comparable size ports in Latin America;
(c) the share of dry bulk cargo would increase from the current 24Z
to 48Z in 2010, while the corresponding shares for conventional
general cargo would be 59Z and 22Z respectively; and
(d) the share of Uruguayan traffic in total MP traffic would decrease
from its current 85-882 to about 49Z.
May 1988

URUGUAY
TRANSPORT PROJECT I
Details of the Implementation and Monitoring of the Roads Component
A.   Implementation of Works in the National Network
1.       The  execution  of  the  highway  replacement  and  rehabilitation
program would be contracted and supervised by the resnonsible directorates
in DNV's organization (Annex 2,  A  (iv)).    DNV would enhance its present
engineering and quality control capacities through the institutional
strengthening program included in the proposed project.
B.   Tmplementation Procedures for Department Maintenance Program
2.       MTOP would serve  as  intermediary  in  the technical preparation,
ece-tomic  evaluation  and  supervision   of  the  department's  maintenance
programs. Participation in the maintenance program of each department
would  be  subject  to  an  agreement  ("convenio")  between  MTOP  and the
department.
3.       The procedures for the execution  of the maintenance programs have
been agreed as follo.ws:
(a) Maintenance program of departmental roads:
Taking into consideration the area, population and present road
network of each department (Table 1), DNV selected, in accordance
with each department, specific gravel roads, totalling about
10,000 km, to receive annually routine maintenance under the
project, at an estimated average cost of US$354 equivalent per km.
Additionally, each department would carry out each year periodic
maintenance of one-fifth of the 'ength of the selected roads, at
an estimated average cost of US$3,215 equivalent per km. The
first year periodic maintenance program comprising 2,000 km has
been agreed and is satisfactory to the Bank. In addition to this
Bank financed maintenance program, the departments would carry out
maintenance of other roads related to milk and rice production
under an IDB financed program, and occasional maintenance of the
remaining road network under c;in financing.
(b) Engineering:
Wherever the programmed  works  require  engineering,  it is being
prepared by the departments with assistance from DNV. Under the
project DNV will assist the departments in upgrading their
engineering capacity.



- 144 -                            ANNEX 9
Page 2 of 4
(c) Execution of the maintenance program:
Execution of works will be accomplished mostly by force account by
the departments, relying partially on contracts for transportation
of materials.    The  technical  capacity  of  most departments is
adequate for the performance of these tasks and will be further
developed through the  technical  assistance  being provided under
the project.   In  some  more  developed  departments, part of the
periodic maintenance works  will  be  contracted.   Contracting of
such works may be increased according to results obtained. The
equipment survey carried out by DNV shows that the departments own
most of the equipment needed for the implementation of the
maintenance program. The contracts with MTOP to be signed by each
department will ensure ready availability of the equipment needed
to carry out the respective program.
(d) Supervision of works and quality control:
DNV will supervise execution of works and compliance with the
agreed  program.    DNV  would  also  assist  the  departments  in
upgrading their soils and materials laboratory facilities and
technologies to improve quality control.
(e) Payment of works executed:
After approvai by Dr-V of  thse waintenarice  w"orks cxcuted bh the
departments and submission  of  specified doculmentation, MTOP will
pay to the department the equivalent of US354 per km of routine
maintenance and the equivalent of US3,215 per km of periodic
maintenance executed. These rates will be reviewed annually and
adjusted according to new average values based on actual costs of
works executed during the previous year.
(f) Agreement between MTOP and the departments:
MTOP will enter into agreements (convenios) with each department
specifying:    Ci)  roads  to  be  maintained  under  the program;
(ii) arrangements with DNV on assistance in engineering and
execution of the program; (iii) arrangements and time schedule for
the acquisition of equipment needed for the execution of the
department's general maintenance program, and which would be also
used in the execution of the Bank financed maintenance program;
(iv) arrangements with DNV for supervision of the maintenance
program and improvement of quality control; (v) arrangements for
payments of works by MTOP to the departments and for review of the
established rates; and (vi) personnel, equipment facilities and
services to be provided by the departments and by DNV and MTOP to
comply with the requirements of  the  agreements.   A draft of the
model agreement between MTOP and the departments was reviewed and
discussed  during  negotiations.       Signing  of  a  satisfactory
agreement with at least 50Z of the departments would be a
condition for effectiveness of the loan. Two conformed copies of
each agreement would be sent to the Bank.



- 145 -                            ANNEX 9
Page 3 of 4
(g) Disbursement of the loan proceeds:
Tbre amount corresponding to the Bank's participation (30?) in the
paymnentr made by MTOP  to  the  departments, would be subsequently
withdrawn by MTOP from the Special Account at the exchange rate
gYevailing at the date of MTOP's payment. MTOP would keep all the
documentation related to these payment and to the execution of the
corresponding works for Bank review.
(h) Loan Repayents, Interest and Other Bank Charges:
MTOP would be responsible for repayment to the 'orld Bank.
'+.      At the completion of the first year maintenance program, MTOP will
be responsible for assessing the performance of each department and
recommend the level of participation in a detailed maintenance program for
the remaining period of  the  loan.    MTOP will apply guidelines developed
during the first year program as part of the maintenance and equipment
management systems analysis to present a plan for the maintenance programs
for the remaining period of the loan. If the review shows the need to
modify the list of roads to be maintained, the eligibility of the new roads
proposed should be approved by the Bank.
C.       Road Project Monitoring
5.       With regard to  project  monitoring,  the  Government will prepare
quarterly reports for the Bank providing information on: (a) the progress
of detailed engineering, bidding and contracting schedules; (b) the
progress of execution of Bank-financed replacement, rehabilitation and
maintenance works; (c) the selection, status of contracts, progress of
execution and expenditures of the proposed gravel roads programs; (d) the
status of disbursements requests; and (e) the schedule of estimated
withdrawals of the proceeds of the loan.
6.       Furthermore, in order to monitor progress on the objectives of the
proposed project, the MTOP will hold semi-annual meetings with Bank staff
around March and September of each year during project implementation to
discuss the following matters and reach agreements when noted:
(a) By September 30 of each year, MTOP will report on:
- proposed update of Five-year Highway Investment Program;
- proposed recurrent and investment budgets for the Natinnal
Highways for upcoming year including definition of additional
works (agreement);
- proposed gravel road maintenance program for the upcoming year
for each participating department (agreement);
- status of implementation of MTOP's Action Plans;
- status of each technical assistance program.



- 146 -                             ANNEX 9
Page 4 of 4
(b) by March 31 of each year, MTOP will report on:
-  actual  road   maintenance,   replacement   and  rehabilitation
expenditures of previous year;
-  actual   gravel   road   maintenance   expenditures   for  each
participating department for previous year;
- maintenance operations for DNV and each department in the
previous year;
- status of replacement, rehabilitation and maintenance budgets
for the current year;
- update of the 5 year road investment plan;
- progress reports for previous year of technical assistance
activities for maintenance management, equipment management,
soils and materials engineering, highway planning and traffic
engineering;
- use of technical assistance funds during previous and estimate
for current year;
- status of implementation of MTOP's actions plans;
-  status of road us.er charges and fuel prices;
September 1988
